<hr style="width: 50%;">
<p><span class="pagenum">[vii]</span></p>
<h2>PREFACE.</h2>
<p>Several years ago, the maker of this version translated into French one
of the early works of H. W. Longfellow. This circumstance was not
forgotten by the American poet who kindly consented to listen to this
new attempt at rendering into English the "CHANSON DE ROLAND."</p>
<p>To his encouragement is due the present publication. The writer will
ever proudly treasure up the remembrance of his friendly welcome and
counsel....</p>
<hr class="two">
<p>The translator has followed, as literally as possible, the text of the
Oxford MS., as revised by Léon Gautier. The parts inclosed in
parentheses are interpolations of the learned Professor. This revised
text should be kept in hand by the English reader for comparison with
the original, which is nine centuries old. The translator may thus be
more likely to obtain the indulgence of the reader for the quaint
representation, in a modern language, of the coloring of this most
ancient poem.</p>
<p><span class="pagenum">[viii]</span>The orthography of all the names, as well as their prosodic accent, has
been preserved in their ancient form; and accordingly, an index has been
appended to the work.</p>
<p>The seventh edition of Léon Gautier's "CHANSON DE ROLAND," contains a
vast amount of explanatory notes, grammatical and historical, to which
the reader is referred.</p>
<h4>HISTORY OF THE POEM.</h4>
<p>On the 15th of August, 778, in a little Pyrenean Valley, still known in
our days by the name of Ronceval, a terrible event took place.
Charlemagne, returning from his expedition to Spain, crossed that valley
and the Pyrenees, leaving his rear-guard in command of Roland, Prefect
of the Marches of Brittany. His main army had passed unmolested; but at
the moment when the rear-guard advanced into the defiles of the
mountain, thousands of Gascons rushed from their ambush, fell upon the
French army and slaughtered the whole guard to the last man. So perished
Roland.</p>
<p>Eginhard, the historian of Charlemagne, terminates his narrative with
these words: "The House-intendant, (Regiæ mensæ præpositus), Eggihard,
Anselm, Count of the Palace, Roland, Prefect of the Marches of Brittany
(Hruolandus britannici limitis præfectus), with many more, perished in
<span class="pagenum">[ix]</span>the fight. It was not possible to take revenge on the spot. The
treacherous attempt once perpetrated, the enemy dispersed and left no
trace." (Eginhard's Life of Charlemagne, Vol. I., p. 31; edition of the
Société de l'histoire de France.)</p>
<p>From the moment of the defeat of Ronceval, legend commenced its labor
upon this truly epic event which, in its origin, is absolutely French,
but has found its echoes throughout Europe, from Iceland to Eastern
regions.</p>
<p>The commentators generally agree in dating the composition of the Poem
before the first crusade in the year 1096. The author, it is
ascertained, was Norman, the dialect used by him being Norman
throughout. Whether this author was really Turoldus, named in the last
line of the Poem, is a point which Léon Gautier refuses to affirm. We
refer the reader to the very interesting preface of <i>Genin</i>, and to the
learned introductions of Léon Gautier, for more complete information.</p>
<hr class="two">
<p>The word "<i>Aoi</i>," which is placed at the end of every stanza, and found in
no other ancient French poems, is interpreted differently by the
commentators. M. Francisque Michel assimilated it at first to the
termination of an ecclesiastical chant—Preface, xxvii.—and later to
the Saxon <i>Abeg</i>, or the English <i>Away</i>, as a sort of refrain which the
"<i>jongleur</i>" repeated at the end of the couplets. M. <span class="pagenum">[x]</span>Génin explains it by
<i>ad viam</i>, a vei, avoie, away! it is done, let us go on!</p>
<p>M. Gautier, with his skeptical honesty, declares the word unexplained.
See Note 9, p. 4, of his seventh edition.</p>
<hr class="two">
<h4>MANUSCRIPTS.</h4>
<p>The most complete and ancient is that of Oxford, in the Bodleian
Library, marked "Digby, 23," a copy of the XIIth. century. All others
are <i>Rifaccimenti</i>, Refashionings.</p>
<p>Two in Venice, in St. Mark Library, XIIIth. century; French MSS., No. 4
&amp; 7.</p>
<p>In the National Library, Paris, No. 860, XIIIth. century.</p>
<p>The Versailles MS., now deposited in the Library of Chateauroux, a copy
of which is in Paris Nat. Library; 15, 108; XIIIth. century.</p>
<p>In the Lyons Library, 964; XIVth. century.</p>
<p>In Cambridge, Trinity Collage, R. 3-32; XVIth. century.</p>
<p>One called the Lorrain, a fragment found near Metz.</p>
<p>The Karlomagnus Saga, an Icelandic copy of the Oxford MS.; XIIIth.
century.</p>
<hr class="two">
<p>In M. Petit de Julleville's Introduction to his version can be found a
chronological list of the <span class="pagenum">[xi]</span>works which concern the "CHANSON DE ROLAND,"
the translations of it, and dissertations on the subject in France and
Germany.</p>
<hr class="two">
<p>There are twenty-one translations in different languages:</p>
<p>Four in German, by Th. Müller, Hertz, Boehmer, Eug. Kölbing.</p>
<p>One in Polish, by Mad. Duchinska.</p>
<p>One in Danish, by Unger.</p>
<p>One in Icelandish, Karlomagnus' Saga.</p>
<p>Twelve in French, by Francisque Michel, Bourdillon, Delécluze, Génin, P.
Paris, Vitet, Jônain, de Saint-Albin, d'Avril, Petit de Julleville,
Lehugeur and Léon Gautier, of whose translation seven editions were
issued.</p>
<p>Two in English, one in England by J. O'Hagan, and one in America, the
latest and present one.</p>
<p>Besides, a version from Vitet's French paraphrase, by Mrs. Marsh.</p>
<hr class="two">
<p><span class="pagenum">[1]</span></p>
<h4>SARRAGOSSA.</h4>
<h4>COUNCIL HELD BY KING MARSILE.</h4>
<h4> <a id="St_1">I.</a></h4>
<div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">Carle our most noble Emperor and King,</span>
<span class="i0">Hath tarried now full seven years in Spain,</span>
<span class="i0">Conqu'ring the highland regions to the sea;</span>
<span class="i0">No fortress stands before him unsubdued,</span>
<span class="i0">Nor wall, nor city left, to be destroyed,</span>
<span class="i0">Save Sarraguce, high on a mountain set.</span>
<span class="i0">There rules the King Marsile who loves not God,</span>
<span class="i0">Apollo worships and Mohammed serves;</span>
<span class="i0">Nor can he from his evil doom escape.</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span>
</div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_2">II.</a></h4><div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">The King Marsile abides in Sarraguce</span>
<span class="i0">Where underneath an orchard's leafy shade,</span>
<span class="i0">Upon a terrace with blue marble paved</span>
<span class="pagenum">[2]</span><span class="i0">He rests. Around him twenty thousand men</span>
<span class="i0">And more are ranked. His Dukes and Counts he calls:</span>
<span class="i0">"Oyez, <i>Seigneurs</i>, what gath'ring ills are ours:</span>
<span class="i0">Great Carle, the Emperor who rules Sweet France</span>
<span class="i0">Comes to this land to 'whelm us with his might.</span>
<span class="i0">To give him battle I no army have,</span>
<span class="i0">Nor people to array against his host:</span>
<span class="i0">Your counsel give me, Lords, as my wise men,</span>
<span class="i0">And so defend your King from death and shame;"</span>
<span class="i0">But answer none a single Pagan gave,</span>
<span class="i0">Save Blancandrin <i>del Castel Val-Fondé</i>.</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span>
</div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_3">III.</a></h4>
<div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">Blancandrin, 'midst the wisest Pagans wise,</span>
<span class="i0">Who, in his vassalage a valiant knight,</span>
<span class="i0">Most prudent counsels gave to help his lord,</span>
<span class="i0">Said to the King:—"Be not by this dismayed!</span>
<span class="i0">To Carle the proud, the fierce, send messengers</span>
<span class="i0">With words of faith and love. Send to him gifts</span>
<span class="i0">Of bears and lions, packs of dogs; present</span>
<span class="i0">Seven hundred camels also, fifty score</span>
<span class="i0">Of molted<a id="FNanchor_1"></a><a class="fnanchor pginternal" href="#Footnote_1">1</a> falcons, and four hundred mules</span>
<span class="i0">With heavy weight of gold and silver packed;</span>
<span class="pagenum">[3]</span><span class="i0">Then fifty chariots with their burthens heaped:</span>
<span class="i0">Well can this treasure all his soldiers pay.</span>
<span class="i0">Within this land he long enough has camped.</span>
<span class="i0">To France—to Aix let him at last return;</span>
<span class="i0">There will you join him on Saint-Michael's feast,</span>
<span class="i0">Accept the Christian law, and swear to be</span>
<span class="i0">His man in faith and honor. Should he ask</span>
<span class="i0">Hostages, ten or twenty grant, to lure</span>
<span class="i0">His trust; let us send our wives' sons. Mine—although</span>
<span class="i0">He die, I give. Far better that their heads</span>
<span class="i0">Should fall than we lose honor and domain,</span>
<span class="i0">Than we ourselves to beggary be brought."</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span>
</div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_4">IV.</a></h4>
<div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">He further said:—"By this right hand of mine,</span>
<span class="i0">And by the beard the air waves on my breast,</span>
<span class="i0">Soon shall you see the host of Franks disperse;</span>
<span class="i0">To France, their land, the Franks will take their way.</span>
<span class="i0">When each has gained the shelter of his home,</span>
<span class="i0">King Carle will in his chapel be at Aix,</span>
<span class="pagenum">[4]</span><span class="i0">To celebrate Saint Michael's solemn feast.</span>
<span class="i0">The day will come, the term allowed will pass,</span>
<span class="i0">And from us shall he hear nor word nor news.</span>
<span class="i0">The King is fierce, his soul is hard; and thus</span>
<span class="i0">Each hostage head beneath his sword shall fall.</span>
<span class="i0">'Twere better far that these should lose their heads</span>
<span class="i0">Than we for aye lose glorious Spain the Fair,</span>
<span class="i0">And suffer so great ills and doleful woes."</span>
<span class="i0">Then say the Pagans:—"This may be the truth."</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span>
</div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_5">V.</a></h4>
<div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">Hereat the King Marsile the council closed.</span>
<span class="i0">Then summon'd he Clarin de Balaguer,</span>
<span class="i0">Estramarin and Eudropin his peer;</span>
<span class="i0">With Priamon Guarlan the bearded knight,</span>
<span class="i0">And Machiner together with Mahen</span>
<span class="i0">His uncle, Joïmer and Malbien born</span>
<span class="i0">Beyond the sea, and Blancandrin, to hear</span>
<span class="i0">His words. These ten, the fiercest, he addressed:</span>
<span class="i0">"Seigneurs Barons, ye shall go toward Carl'magne;</span>
<span class="i0">He to Cordrès, the city, now lays siege.</span>
<span class="i0">Bear in each hand a branch of olive-tree</span>
<span class="i0">In token of humility and peace.</span>
<span class="pagenum">[5]</span>
<span class="i0">If by your arts his favor you can gain,</span>
<span class="i0">I give of gold and silver, lands and fiefs</span>
<span class="i0">To each, whatever he may ask of me."</span>
<span class="i0">The Pagans answer all:—"[Well said our lord!"]</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span>
</div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_6">VI.</a></h4>
<div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">Marsile his council closed:—"My Lords, ye shall</span>
<span class="i0">Set forth;—an olive branch bear in each hand:</span>
<span class="i0">And in my name adjure King Carlemagne</span>
<span class="i0">That by his God he mercy have on me;</span>
<span class="i0">And ere a month be past, he shall behold</span>
<span class="i0">Me follow with a thousand faithful knights,</span>
<span class="i0">There to submit myself to Christian law</span>
<span class="i0">And be his man in love and faith; and if</span>
<span class="i0">He hostages require, them shall he have."</span>
<span class="i0">Quoth Blancandrin:—"Good treaty will be yours."</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span>
</div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_7">VII.</a></h4>
<div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">Marsile then ordered forth the ten white mules</span>
<span class="i0">The King of Sicily once sent to him;—</span>
<span class="i0">Golden their bits—their saddles silver-wrought—</span>
<span class="pagenum">[6]</span><span class="i0">And on them mounted his ambassadors.</span>
<span class="i0">Thus holding each a branch of olive-tree,</span>
<span class="i0">They rode away and came to Carle of France.</span>
<span class="i0">Nor can he from the treacherous snare escape.</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span>
</div></div>
<hr style="width: 50%;">
<p><span class="pagenum">[7]</span></p>
<h4>COUNCIL OF CHARLEMAGNE AT<br>
CORDOBA.</h4>
<h4> <a id="St_8">VIII.</a></h4>
<div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">Cheerful and blithe the Emp'ror, for Cordrès</span>
<span class="i0">Has been subdued, its massy walls o'erthrown,</span>
<span class="i0">Its towers by mighty catapults destroyed;</span>
<span class="i0">And there his knights have found abundant spoils</span>
<span class="i0">Of gold and silver, and rich garnitures.</span>
<span class="i0">Nor was one Pagan in the city left</span>
<span class="i0">Alive, who did not own the Christian Faith.</span>
<span class="i0">Now is the Emperor within a wide</span>
<span class="i0">And spreading orchard; there around him stand</span>
<span class="i0">Rollánd and Olivier, Samsun the Duke,</span>
<span class="i0">And Anseïs the bold, Gefrei d'Anjou,</span>
<span class="i0">Gonfaloneer of Carle, and also there</span>
<span class="i0">Gerin and Gerier. Where these were, came</span>
<span class="i0">Of others many more. In all, from France</span>
<span class="pagenum">[8]</span><span class="i0">Were gathered fully fifteen thousand knights.</span>
<span class="i0">Upon white <i>pallies</i><a id="FNanchor_2"></a><a class="fnanchor pginternal" href="#Footnote_2">2</a> sit these chevaliers;</span>
<span class="i0">They play at tables<a id="FNanchor_3"></a><a class="fnanchor pginternal" href="#Footnote_3">3</a> to divert themselves;</span>
<span class="i0">The wiser and the elder play at chess.</span>
<span class="i0">In mimic sword-play strive the joyous youths.</span>
<span class="i0">Under a pine-tree, near an eglantine,</span>
<span class="i0">Is placed a faldstool of pure gold whereon</span>
<span class="i0">Sits he, the King—great Ruler of Sweet France.</span>
<span class="i0">White is his beard, his head all flowering white;</span>
<span class="i0">Graceful his form and proud his countenance;</span>
<span class="i0">None need to point him out to those who come</span>
<span class="i0">The Pagan messengers, dismounting, stood</span>
<span class="i0">Before him, proffering humble faith and love.</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span>
</div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_9">IX.</a></h4>
<div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">Blancandrin was the first to speak, and said</span>
<span class="i0">Unto the king:—"Hail! in the name of God,</span>
<span class="i0">The Glorious One we must adore! To you</span>
<span class="i0">I bring this message from Marsile the brave:</span>
<span class="i0">Well has he studied your Salvation's Law;</span>
<span class="i0">And would upon you lavish his great wealth.</span>
<span class="pagenum">[9]</span><span class="i0">Bears—lions—packs of hounds enchained he gives,</span>
<span class="i0">Seven hundred camels also—fifty score</span>
<span class="i0">Of molted falcons—mules, four hundred, packed</span>
<span class="i0">With gold and silver—fifty carts to carry</span>
<span class="i0">These gifts, and <i>bezants</i><a id="FNanchor_4"></a><a class="fnanchor pginternal" href="#Footnote_4">4</a> of the purest gold</span>
<span class="i0">He also sends, which will your soldiers pay.</span>
<span class="i0">Too long within our land you have remained;</span>
<span class="i0">To France—to Aix he wills you straight return.</span>
<span class="i0">There will he follow you: so says my lord."</span>
<span class="i0">To God the Emperor uplifts his hands;</span>
<span class="i0">Bends low his head and counsel takes in thought.</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span>
</div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_10">X.</a></h4>
<div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">The Emperor sat silent with drooped head.</span>
<span class="i0">Ne'er rash in words, he never speaks in haste.</span>
<span class="i0">At last he rose. Proudly he looked and spake</span>
<span class="i0">Unto the messengers:—"Ye have well said</span>
<span class="i0">That King Marsile e'er stood my greatest foe!</span>
<span class="i0">On these fair-seeming words how far can I</span>
<span class="i0">Rely?" The crafty Saracen replied:</span>
<span class="pagenum">[10]</span><span class="i0">"Would you have hostages? you shall have ten,</span>
<span class="i0">Fifteen, yea, twenty. Though his fate be death</span>
<span class="i0">My son shall go, and others nobler still,</span>
<span class="i0">I deem. When to your lordly palace, home</span>
<span class="i0">Returned—when comes Saint Michael del Peril,</span>
<span class="i0">His feast, my Lord will follow to those springs,</span>
<span class="i0">He says, brought forth by God for you, and there</span>
<span class="i0">Baptized, a faithful Christian will become!"</span>
<span class="i0">Carl'magne makes answer:—"He may yet be saved!"</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span>
</div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_11">XI.</a></h4>
<div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">The eve was soft and fair, the sunset bright;</span>
<span class="i0">The ten mules stabled by the King's command;</span>
<span class="i0">In the great orchard a pavilion raised</span>
<span class="i0">To house the messengers, his Pagan guests.</span>
<span class="i0">Twelve sergeants to their service were assigned,</span>
<span class="i0">And there they rested till the dawn repelled</span>
<span class="i0">The night. With day the Emperor arose;</span>
<span class="i0">Heard mass and matins first, then having gone</span>
<span class="i0">Beneath a stately pine, he summons all</span>
<span class="i0">His wisest barons, council grave to hold,</span>
<span class="i0">Thus ever guided by the men of France.</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span>
</div></div>
<p><span class="pagenum">[11]</span></p>
<h4> <a id="St_12">XII.</a></h4>
<div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">Beneath a pine the Monarch has repaired;</span>
<span class="i0">His barons to the council called: the Duke</span>
<span class="i0">Ogier—Archbishop Turpin—old Richard,</span>
<span class="i0">Also his nephew Henri, and the brave</span>
<span class="i0">Count Acelin of Gascuïgne—Tedbald</span>
<span class="i0">De Reins—his cousin Milon—Gerier,</span>
<span class="i0">Gerin, together with the Count Rollánd,</span>
<span class="i0">And Olivier, the brave and noble knight.</span>
<span class="i0">One thousand Franks of France and more were met—</span>
<span class="i0">Then Ganelon came, who treason wrought; and now</span>
<span class="i0">Was opened that ill-fated council thus:</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span>
</div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_13">XIII.</a></h4>
<div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">"Seigneurs Barons," began the Emp'ror Carle,</span>
<span class="i0">"The King Marsile his messengers hath sent</span>
<span class="i0">To offer me large store of his great wealth;</span>
<span class="i0">Bears—lions—hounds in leash;—of camels he</span>
<span class="i0">Gives seven hundred—falcons, fifty score—</span>
<span class="i0">Four hundred mules packed high with Arab gold,</span>
<span class="i0">And more than fifty chariots loaded full;</span>
<span class="pagenum">[12]</span><span class="i0">But he demands that I return to France.</span>
<span class="i0">There will he follow: then arrived at Aix,</span>
<span class="i0">Will in my palace take Salvation's Faith,</span>
<span class="i0">Will Christ obey, and hold his lands from me;</span>
<span class="i0">But what is in his heart, I do not know."</span>
<span class="i0">The French exclaim:—"Of him we must beware!"</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span>
</div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_14">XIV.</a></h4>
<div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">The Emp'ror ended thus. But Count Rollánd</span>
<span class="i0">Approving not the terms, stands forth and speaks</span>
<span class="i0">Unto the King with arguments adverse:</span>
<span class="i0">"Trust never more Marsile. 'Tis full seven years</span>
<span class="i0">Since we came into Spain. For you I took</span>
<span class="i0">Both Noplés and Commiblés; gained Valterne</span>
<span class="i0">And all the land of Pine, and Balaguer,</span>
<span class="i0">And Tuele and Sebile—yet King Marsile</span>
<span class="i0">Still plotting treachery, sent from his horde</span>
<span class="i0">Of Pagans, fifteen men; each bore in hand</span>
<span class="i0">Like these, a branch of olive-tree, and spake</span>
<span class="i0">The self-same words. On that you counsel took</span>
<span class="i0">From your too lightly flattering French; two Counts</span>
<span class="i0">Of yours you to the Pagan sent, the one,</span>
<span class="i0">Bazan, Bastile the other, and their heads</span>
<span class="i0">He struck off near Haltoie. As you began,</span>
<span class="pagenum">[13]</span><span class="i0">War on! To Sarraguce your army lead,</span>
<span class="i0">Besiege her walls, though all your life it take,</span>
<span class="i0">And thus avenge the knights the felon slew."</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span>
</div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_15">XV.</a></h4>
<div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">At this the Emperor, bending low his head,</span>
<span class="i0">Twists his mustache and plucks his hoary beard,</span>
<span class="i0">Answering his nephew neither yea nor nay.</span>
<span class="i0">The Franks keep silence—all save Ganelon</span>
<span class="i0">Who rose and stood before the King, and spake</span>
<span class="i0">Bold words and haughty:—"Put not faith in fools,</span>
<span class="i0">Nor me nor others; follow your own rede!</span>
<span class="i0">Since King Marsile makes offer to become</span>
<span class="i0">Your man, with hands joined; furthermore will hold</span>
<span class="i0">Spain as a fief from you; yea, will receive</span>
<span class="i0">Our law as his law, he who counsel gives</span>
<span class="i0">Such proffer to reject, cares not a whit</span>
<span class="i0">What death we die. No counsel take of pride;</span>
<span class="i0">Let pass the fools and listen to the wise."</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span>
</div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_16">XVI.</a></h4>
<div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">And now Duke Naimes arose: his beard and hair</span>
<span class="i0">As white as drifted snow. In all the court</span>
<span class="pagenum">[14]</span><span class="i0">No better vassal stood; and to the King:</span>
<span class="i0">"Have you marked well the words Count Ganelon</span>
<span class="i0">In answer spoke but now? His plan is wise;</span>
<span class="i0">Follow it then. This King Marsile in war</span>
<span class="i0">Is overcome, his strongholds all pulled down;</span>
<span class="i0">By warlike engines are his walls destroyed,</span>
<span class="i0">His cities burned, his men subdued;—when now</span>
<span class="i0">He for your mercy prays, foul sin it were</span>
<span class="i0">To press him harder. Since he, furthermore</span>
<span class="i0">Will bind his word by gift of hostages,</span>
<span class="i0">[One of your barons also send to him.]</span>
<span class="i0">In truth no longer this great war should rage."</span>
<span class="i0">The French all cry:—"Duke Naimes has spoken well."</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span>
</div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_17">XVII.</a></h4>
<div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">"Seigneurs Barons, which of you shall we send</span>
<span class="i0">To meet the King Marsile in Sarraguce?"</span>
<span class="i0">Duke Naimes responds:—"I, with your leave will go;</span>
<span class="i0">Give me the glove and staff."—"Nay," quoth King Carle,</span>
<span class="i0">"A sage you are in council, well I know:</span>
<span class="i0">By this mustache and by this beard of mine,</span>
<span class="i0">So far away from me you shall not go.</span>
<span class="i0">Back to your seat, since none hath summoned you."</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span>
</div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_18">XVIII.</a></h4>
<div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="pagenum">[15]</span>
<span class="i0">"Seigneurs Barons, which of you shall we send</span>
<span class="i0">As messengers to Sarraguce where rules</span>
<span class="i0">Marsile?"—Rollánd responds:—"Behold me here!"</span>
<span class="i0">"—You shall not, by my troth!" cries Olivier,</span>
<span class="i0">"Your pride too fierce, and courage far too hot;</span>
<span class="i0">I fear some misadventure from your zeal.</span>
<span class="i0">Should our King grant me but his leave, 'tis I</span>
<span class="i0">Will go!"—The King exclaimed:—"Be silent both—</span>
<span class="i0">Nor you, nor he, shall yonder set your foot!</span>
<span class="i0">Ay, by this hoary beard of mine, I swear,</span>
<span class="i0">Not one of my twelve Peers shall thither go."</span>
<span class="i0">The French are dumb—-all silenced by these words.</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span>
</div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_19">XIX.</a></h4>
<div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">Turpin de Reins arises from the ranks</span>
<span class="i0">And to the King he says: "Let your Franks stay,</span>
<span class="i0">To this land seven years ago you came,</span>
<span class="i0">And they have suffered much of toil and pain.</span>
<span class="i0">Give me the glove and staff, and I will go</span>
<span class="pagenum">[16]</span><span class="i0">And speak my mind to that proud Saracen."</span>
<span class="i0">With anger great the Emperor replies:</span>
<span class="i0">"Back to your seat on yonder <i>pallie</i> white</span>
<span class="i0">Nor speak another word, save by command!"</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span>
</div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_20">XX.</a></h4>
<div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">Then said the Emperor:—"Chevaliers of France,</span>
<span class="i0">Choose ye for me a baron of my realm,</span>
<span class="i0">One who can bear my words to King Marsile!"</span>
<span class="i0">Rollánd rejoins:—"Let my step-father go;</span>
<span class="i0">If he remain, no wiser man is found."</span>
<span class="i0">The French say:—-"Well can he fulfill the task:</span>
<span class="i0">[If the King wills, 'tis right he should be sent."]</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span>
</div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_21">XXI.</a></h4>
<div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">Thus spoke the King:—"Sire Ganelon, draw near:</span>
<span class="i0">Receive the glove and staff—you heard the Franks</span>
<span class="i0">Pass judgment, and on you their choice has fallen."</span>
<span class="i0">Said Ganelon:—"All this Rollánd has done!</span>
<span class="i0">My life-long, never will I love him more,</span>
<span class="i0">Nor Olivier, his comrade and his friend,</span>
<span class="i0">Nor the twelve Peers, for that they love him well.</span>
<span class="i0">Here in your presence, I defy them all!"</span>
<span class="pagenum">[17]</span><span class="i0">The King replied:—"Too wroth you are. At once</span>
<span class="i0">You shall depart.—I spoke it."—"Sire, I go,</span>
<span class="i0">Although for me there is nor shield nor guard:</span>
<span class="i0">Basile had none, Bazan, his brother, none!</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span>
</div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_22">XXII.</a></h4>
<div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">"To Sarraguce I go, and know full well</span>
<span class="i0">Who thither goes, may ne'er return. Nay more,</span>
<span class="i0">Your sister is my wife, and I by her</span>
<span class="i0">Have one fair son, Baldewin, the goodliest child</span>
<span class="i0">Who [if he live] will be a noble knight.</span>
<span class="i0">To him I leave my fiefs and honors: guard</span>
<span class="i0">Him well, for him these eyes no more shall see."</span>
<span class="i0">Carle answers:—"Much too tender is your heart;</span>
<span class="i0">Since I command, your duty 'tis to go."</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span>
</div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_23">XXIII.</a></h4>
<div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">Count Ganelon, at this, rose full of wrath,</span>
<span class="i0">And, casting from his neck his zibelline</span>
<span class="i0">Of fur, stood forth, clothed in his silk <i>blialt.</i><a id="FNanchor_5"></a><a class="fnanchor pginternal" href="#Footnote_5">5</a></span>
<span class="i0">Gray were his eyes and very fierce his face;</span>
<span class="i0">Graceful his form—his breast, of mighty mold.</span>
<span class="i0">So fair was he, all eyes upon him rest.</span>
<span class="pagenum">[18]</span><span class="i0">"Rollánd," he said, "wherefore this foolish wrath?</span>
<span class="i0">Since thy step-father, 'tis well known, I am,</span>
<span class="i0">For this thou choosest me to seek Marsile!</span>
<span class="i0">'Tis well. If God but grant me safe return,</span>
<span class="i0">I such ill fortune hurl on thee, shall smite</span>
<span class="i0">Thy life from now and ever with a curse."</span>
<span class="i0">Rollánd replies:—"Mad words and proud I hear.</span>
<span class="i0">All know it well, I care for no man's threat;</span>
<span class="i0">But since a wise man must this message bear,</span>
<span class="i0">If the King wills it, in your place I go."</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span>
</div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_24">XXIV.</a></h4>
<div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">"Thou shalt not take my place," said Ganelon;</span>
<span class="i0">"My vassal art thou not, nor yet am I</span>
<span class="i0">Thy lord; and since the King hath given me</span>
<span class="i0">Command this service I should take, I shall</span>
<span class="i0">Go to Marsile. But once in Sarraguce</span>
<span class="i0">Will I with fuel feed my heart's fierce ire."</span>
<span class="i0">Rollánd, on hearing this, began to laugh.</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span>
</div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_25">XXV.</a></h4>
<div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">When Ganelon saw the laughter of Rollánd,</span>
<span class="i0">It seemed as though his breast would burst with wrath;</span>
<span class="pagenum">[19]</span>
<span class="i0">His brain was well-nigh maddened by his rage.</span>
<span class="i0">Unto the Count he cried:—"I love you not;</span>
<span class="i0">This judgment have you caused on me to fall!</span>
<span class="i0">Right Emp'ror, in your presence, lo! I stand,</span>
<span class="i0">And I am ready to fulfill your word."</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span>
</div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_26">XXVI.</a></h4>
<div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">The King presents to him his right hand glove;</span>
<span class="i0">But Ganelon would well have ne'er stood there,</span>
<span class="i0">For ere he touched the royal glove, it fell.</span>
<span class="i0">The French exclaim:—"What bodes this omen? Shall</span>
<span class="i0">This embassy not have a woeful end?—"</span>
<span class="i0">"<i>Seigneurs</i>," said Ganelon, "you will hear of this!"</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span>
</div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_27">XXVII.</a></h4>
<div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">Said Ganelon:—"Give me dismissal, Sire!</span>
<span class="i0">Since I must go, my time is precious." Then</span>
<span class="i0">Adjured the King:—"For Jesus' sake and mine!"</span>
<span class="i0">With his right hand he Ganelon absolved</span>
<span class="i0">And blessed, deliv'ring up the brief and staff.</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span>
</div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_28">XXVIII.</a></h4><div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="pagenum">[20]</span>
<span class="i0">Count Ganelon his own house seeks, to make</span>
<span class="i0">Equipment and prepare his arms: his choice</span>
<span class="i0">The best that he can find. With golden spurs</span>
<span class="i0">He clasps his heels; belts to his side his sword,</span>
<span class="i0">Murgleis, and mounts his courser Tachebrun.</span>
<span class="i0">His uncle Guinemer the stirrup held;</span>
<span class="i0">There many a chevalier you might have seen</span>
<span class="i0">In tears, who said: "Baron, such evil fate</span>
<span class="i0">Was yours. You, in the King's Court so long, and there</span>
<span class="i0">Revered as liege-man high!—The man who judged</span>
<span class="i0">That you should go, not Carle himself shall cure</span>
<span class="i0">Or save; the Count Rollánd bethought him not</span>
<span class="i0">Of that high lineage whence you sprang!"—And they</span>
<span class="i0">Entreat:—"My lord with you take us along!"</span>
<span class="i0">But Ganelon replies:—"Lord God forbid!</span>
<span class="i0">Better to die alone than with me fall</span>
<span class="i0">So many brave!—Lords, to sweet France ye will go.</span>
<span class="i0">Salute for me my wife, and Pinabel,</span>
<span class="i0">My friend and peer, and my son Baldewin whom</span>
<span class="i0">Ye all know—guard him—hold him for your lord."</span>
<span class="i0">The Count departs and goes upon his way.</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span>
</div></div>
<hr style="width: 50%;">
<p><span class="pagenum">[21]</span></p>
<h4>THE EMBASSY AND CRIME OF GANELON.</h4>
<h4> <a id="St_29">XXIX.</a></h4>
<div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">The Count rides on beneath tall olive trees</span>
<span class="i0">And joins the messengers of King Marsile.</span>
<span class="i0">To meet him Blancandrin has checked his speed:</span>
<span class="i0">With skillful words each to the other spake.</span>
<span class="i0">Blancandrin said: "A wond'rous man is Carle</span>
<span class="i0">Who conquered Pouille and overran Calabre,</span>
<span class="i0">Crossed the salt-seas to England, and from thence</span>
<span class="i0">Gained tribute for <i>Saint-Pierre</i>. In this our land</span>
<span class="i0">What claims he?" "Such his might," said Ganelon,</span>
<span class="i0">"No man shall ever match with him in arms."</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span>
</div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_30">XXX.</a></h4>
<div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">Said Blancandrin: "The Franks are noble, but</span>
<span class="i0">Those Dukes, those Counts harm much their lord, who give</span>
<span class="pagenum">[22]</span><span class="i0">To him such counsels, wronging him and all."</span>
<span class="i0">Ganelon answered: "No man, save Rollánd,</span>
<span class="i0">Know I, who should this blame incur; it was</span>
<span class="i0">But yestermorn, the King sat in the shade,</span>
<span class="i0">When Rollánd came before him, all encased</span>
<span class="i0">In glittering arms, fresh from the siege and sack</span>
<span class="i0">Of Carcasonne, holding an apple red;</span>
<span class="i0">And thus his uncle greeted: 'Sire, behold!</span>
<span class="i0">I lay the crowns of all Kings at your feet.'</span>
<span class="i0">Swift punishment should overtake such pride,</span>
<span class="i0">For ev'ry day he blindly runs to death.</span>
<span class="i0">Were he but slain, all lands might rest in peace."</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span>
</div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_31">XXXI.</a></h4>
<div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">Blancandrin said: "Most cruel is Rollánd</span>
<span class="i0">Who makes all nations cry for mercy thus,</span>
<span class="i0">And will o'er all the lands his power impose.</span>
<span class="i0">Upon what people doth he then rely</span>
<span class="i0">For such attempt?" Ganelon said: "The French!...</span>
<span class="i0">They love him so, they fail him ne'er in aught.</span>
<span class="i0">Lavish is he of gifts: Silver and gold,</span>
<span class="i0">Mules, chargers, silken robes and garnitures,</span>
<span class="i0">He gives the King himself all that he craves;</span>
<span class="i0">From here to the far East, all lands must fall!"</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span>
</div></div>
<p><span class="pagenum">[23]</span></p>
<h4> <a id="St_32">XXXII.</a></h4>
<div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">Blancandrin with Count Ganelon rode on,</span>
<span class="i0">Until together had they pledged their faith</span>
<span class="i0">To snare Rollánd and lead him to his death.</span>
<span class="i0">Thus on they rode through vales and mountain-paths,</span>
<span class="i0">Till Sarraguce was reached. Beneath a yew</span>
<span class="i0">They lighted: a faldstool by shady pines</span>
<span class="i0">O'erhung, was spread with Alexandrine silk.</span>
<span class="i0">There sat the King who ruled all Spain, and stood</span>
<span class="i0">Around him twenty thousand Saracens,</span>
<span class="i0">Who neither spoke nor breathed, to hear the news;</span>
<span class="i0">And lo! came Blancandrin with Ganelon.</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span>
</div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_33">XXXIII.</a></h4>
<div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">Blancandrin stepped before the Pagan King</span>
<span class="i0">With Ganelon the Count held by the wrist.</span>
<span class="i0">Thus to Marsile he said: "Mohammed save</span>
<span class="i0">The King! Apollo, too, whose holy law</span>
<span class="i0">We keep. We bore your message to Carl'magne;</span>
<span class="i0">Both hands he lifted, praying to his God;</span>
<span class="i0">No other answer gave.—He sends you here</span>
<span class="pagenum">[24]</span><span class="i0">One of his noble Barons, a rich Frank.</span>
<span class="i0">Learn from his lips if it be peace or war."</span>
<span class="i0">Responds Marsile: "Then let him speak. We hear!"</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span>
</div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_34">XXXIV.</a></h4>
<div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">Then Ganelon, who well had weighed his thoughts,</span>
<span class="i0">Begins to speak as one of knowledge vast,</span>
<span class="i0">And says unto the King: "By God be saved,</span>
<span class="i0">The Glorious God we must adore! Carl'magne</span>
<span class="i0">The Baron, sends his message to Marsile:</span>
<span class="i0">The holy Christian Faith if you receive,</span>
<span class="i0">One half of Spain he grants to you in fief.</span>
<span class="i0">These terms refuse, and your fair Sarraguce</span>
<span class="i0">He will besiege, and drag you forth in chains</span>
<span class="i0">To Aix, his royal city, there to meet</span>
<span class="i0">A felon's doom."—Quivering with rage and fear,</span>
<span class="i0">The King Marsile, who held a gold-winged dart,</span>
<span class="i0">Aims it at him; but others stayed his hand.</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span>
</div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_35">XXXV.</a></h4>
<div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">The King Marsile turned pale, and full of wrath,</span>
<span class="i0">Brandished the shaft of his winged dart on high.</span>
<span class="i0">Ganelon saw, laid hand upon his sword,</span>
<span class="pagenum">[25]</span><span class="i0">And quick unsheathed two fingers' breadth of blade,</span>
<span class="i0">Saying: "Sword of mine you are most fair and bright;</span>
<span class="i0">As long as by me borne in this King's court,</span>
<span class="i0">Never shall say the Emperor of France</span>
<span class="i0">Ganelon died alone in foreign land,</span>
<span class="i0">Ere a high price for you the best have paid!"</span>
<span class="i0">The Pagans cry in haste: "Check this affray."</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span>
</div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_36">XXXVI.</a></h4>
<div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">The wisest Pagans urged the wrathful King,</span>
<span class="i0">Till, yielding, on his throne he has resumed</span>
<span class="i0">His seat. The Kalif said: "Great wrong you brought</span>
<span class="i0">Upon us, menacing to strike the Frank.</span>
<span class="i0">You should have hearkened to his words." "This wrong,"</span>
<span class="i0">Said Ganelon, "I calmly will endure;</span>
<span class="i0">But for the gold that God hath made all wealth</span>
<span class="i0">Stored in this land, I would not leave untold,</span>
<span class="i0">While I have power of speech, the message sent</span>
<span class="i0">By Carle, the mighty Emperor, through me</span>
<span class="i0">His messenger, to thee his mortal foe."</span>
<span class="i0">Ganelon on the ground his mantle dropped</span>
<span class="i0">Of Alexandrine silk, and richly lined</span>
<span class="pagenum">[26]</span><span class="i0">With zibelline; Blancandrin took it up:</span>
<span class="i0">But from his sword the Count would never part;</span>
<span class="i0">And his right hand still grasps the golden hilt.</span>
<span class="i0">The Pagans say:—"Behold a Baron true!"</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span>
</div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_37">XXXVII.</a></h4>
<div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">Then Ganelon strode nearer to the King</span>
<span class="i0">And said:—"All idle is this wrath of yours.</span>
<span class="i0">This is the message of King Carle of France;</span>
<span class="i0">Hear his command:—"Receive the Christian law"—</span>
<span class="i0">One half of Spain he grants to you in fief,</span>
<span class="i0">And to Rollánd, his nephew, he will give</span>
<span class="i0">The other half. (A haughty partner he</span>
<span class="i0">Will prove.) To this agreement should you not</span>
<span class="i0">Consent, 'gainst Sarraguce his host will lay</span>
<span class="i0">The siege; by force you will be tak'n and bound,</span>
<span class="i0">And brought to Aix, the royal seat. Hope not</span>
<span class="i0">To ride on palfrey, nor on steed, on mule</span>
<span class="i0">Female or male;—on a vile beast of burden</span>
<span class="i0">You shall be thrown, and doomed to have your head</span>
<span class="i0">Struck off.—Behold the Brief our Emp'ror sends!"</span>
<span class="i0">With his right hand he gives it to the King.</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span>
</div></div>
<p><span class="pagenum">[27]</span></p>
<h4> <a id="St_38">XXXVIII.</a></h4>
<div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">White with exceeding wrath, the King Marsile</span>
<span class="i0">Has brok'n the seal, let fall the wax on earth,</span>
<span class="i0">And, glancing on the Brief, has read the script:</span>
<span class="i0">"I learn from Carle who holds France in his sway,</span>
<span class="i0">That I should bear in mind his ire and grief:</span>
<span class="i0">Bazan—Basile, his brother, they whose heads</span>
<span class="i0">I took on Mount Haltoïe, his anger's cause.</span>
<span class="i0">If I my body's life would save, to him</span>
<span class="i0">The Kalif, my good uncle, I must send,</span>
<span class="i0">Or else can he ne'er be my friend."—Then spake</span>
<span class="i0">To King Marsile his son:—"This Ganelon,"</span>
<span class="i0">Said he, "speaks madly, and such wrong hath done,</span>
<span class="i0">That he should live no more. Now give him up</span>
<span class="i0">To me, that I to him quick justice deal!"</span>
<span class="i0">Ganelon, hearing this, unsheathed his sword,</span>
<span class="i0">And set his back against a branching pine.</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span>
</div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_39">XXXIX.</a></h4>
<div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">Into his orchard King Marsile repaired,</span>
<span class="i0">Attended only by his wisest men;</span>
<span class="i0">Came thither too the gray-haired Blancandrin</span>
<span class="pagenum">[28]</span><span class="i0">With Turfaleu his son and heir; with them</span>
<span class="i0">The Kalif, brother and good friend of King</span>
<span class="i0">Marsile.—Said Blancandrin:—"Recall the Frank;</span>
<span class="i0">To serve us he has pledged his faith."—The King</span>
<span class="i0">Replied:—"Go, bring him hither."—Then he took</span>
<span class="i0">Ganelon's fingers into his right hand,</span>
<span class="i0">And brought him to the grove before the King;</span>
<span class="i0">And lo! was woven there the traitor's plot.</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span>
</div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_40">XL.</a></h4>
<div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">The King Marsile said:—"Fair Sire Ganelon,</span>
<span class="i0">Unwise and all too hasty was I, when</span>
<span class="i0">In my great wrath I poised my lance to strike.</span>
<span class="i0">This gift of sables take as your amends:</span>
<span class="i0">More than five hundred marks their weight in gold.</span>
<span class="i0">Before to-morrow-eve the boon is yours."</span>
<span class="i0">Ganelon answers:—"I reject it not.</span>
<span class="i0">May God, if 'tis his will, your grace reward."</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span>
</div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_41">XLI.</a></h4>
<div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">Marsile spake thus:—"Sire Ganelon, believe,</span>
<span class="i0">Much I desire to love you, and of Carle</span>
<span class="pagenum">[29]</span>
<span class="i0">I crave to hear. Is he not old, his prime</span>
<span class="i0">Has he not passed? Men tell me he has lived</span>
<span class="i0">More than two hundred years; his body dragged</span>
<span class="i0">Throughout so many lands; so many blows</span>
<span class="i0">Upon his shield!—So many mighty Kings</span>
<span class="i0">To beggary reduced!—When will he cease</span>
<span class="i0">To march on battle-fields?"—Then Ganelon</span>
<span class="i0">Responded:—"Such is not King Carle; no man</span>
<span class="i0">Alive who sees and knows him but will tell</span>
<span class="i0">How our great Emperor is Baron true.</span>
<span class="i0">I could not praise and honor him enough,</span>
<span class="i0">For no man lives so valiant and so good.</span>
<span class="i0">His valor ... who on earth could ever tell?</span>
<span class="i0">His soul God with such virtue has illumed,</span>
<span class="i0">I'd rather die than quit my noble lord!"</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span>
</div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_42">XLII.</a></h4>
<div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">The Pagan said:—"Amazed am I at Carle</span>
<span class="i0">So old and so white-haired; his age, I know,</span>
<span class="i0">Two hundred years and more. His limbs he toiled</span>
<span class="i0">Across so many lands; so oft was struck</span>
<span class="i0">By swords and spears; so many kings compelled</span>
<span class="i0">To beg!—When will he cease to war?"—"Carle?—ne'er!"</span>
<span class="pagenum">[30]</span><span class="i0">Ganelon answered, "while his nephew lives:</span>
<span class="i0">No vassal like him 'neath the starry arch;</span>
<span class="i0">And bold as he his comrade Olivier.</span>
<span class="i0">The twelve Peers held by Carle so dear, behold!</span>
<span class="i0">The vanguard form of twenty thousand knights;</span>
<span class="i0">With them King Carle is safe, and fears no man."</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span>
</div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_43">XLIII.</a></h4>
<div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">Again the Pagan:—"I am wonder-struck</span>
<span class="i0">On knowing Carle so old and so white-haired!</span>
<span class="i0">Methinks he passed two hundred years; by arms</span>
<span class="i0">He won so many lands—so many wounds</span>
<span class="i0">In battle he received from trenchant swords!</span>
<span class="i0">So many powerful kings on battle-fields</span>
<span class="i0">Conquered or slew!—When will he cease to war?"</span>
<span class="i0">"—Never!"—said Ganelon, "while lives Rollánd:</span>
<span class="i0">From here to farthest east no knight his peer</span>
<span class="i0">E'er lived: his comrade too, Count Olivier,</span>
<span class="i0">Is brave; and the twelve Peers, so dear to Carle,</span>
<span class="i0">The van-guard make of twenty thousand knights.</span>
<span class="i0">Carle may have peace, and fears no living man!"</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span>
</div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_44">XLIV.</a></h4>
<div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">"Fair Sire," said King Marsile to Ganelon,</span>
<span class="i0">"Than mine no fairer people can you see:</span>
<span class="pagenum">[31]</span><span class="i0">Four hundred thousand knights I can array</span>
<span class="i0">In combat 'gainst King Carle and 'gainst his Franks."—</span>
<span class="i0">Ganelon says:—"The time has not yet come,</span>
<span class="i0">Yea, and great loss your people then will have.</span>
<span class="i0">But leave this folly, and to wisdom hold;</span>
<span class="i0">Offer the King of treasures such a store</span>
<span class="i0">That all the French will marvel at the gift.</span>
<span class="i0">For twenty hostages that you will send,</span>
<span class="i0">Back to Sweet France will Carle ere long repair.</span>
<span class="i0">His rear-guard, notice well, will rest behind:</span>
<span class="i0">There will Rollánd, his nephew, be, I trow,</span>
<span class="i0">With Olivier the brave and courteous knight.</span>
<span class="i0">Trust to my counsel and both Counts are doomed,</span>
<span class="i0">Nay, Carle shall see his lofty pride cast down</span>
<span class="i0">And never more shall covet war with you."</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span>
</div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_45">XLV.</a></h4>
<div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">[Thus King Marsile] said:—"Fair sire Ganelon,</span>
<span class="i0">What means have I to kill the Count Rollànd?"</span>
<span class="i0">Ganelon answered:—"This can I well say:</span>
<span class="i0">The King will reach the wider pass of Sizre</span>
<span class="i0">And leave his rear behind, where great Rollànd</span>
<span class="i0">Eke Olivier, whom both he greatly trusts,</span>
<span class="i0">Will be the chiefs of twenty thousand Franks.</span>
<span class="pagenum">[32]</span><span class="i0">On these your hundred thousand Pagans throw,</span>
<span class="i0">And let them straightway make an onset fierce:</span>
<span class="i0">Stricken and slain shall be the men of France;</span>
<span class="i0">I say not that of yours none shall be slain,</span>
<span class="i0">But follow up this fight with like attack,</span>
<span class="i0">And Count Rollánd cannot escape them both,</span>
<span class="i0">Then will you deeds of chivalry achieve,</span>
<span class="i0">And free your life from war for evermore."</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span>
</div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_46">XLVI.</a></h4>
<div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">"Who could contrive that there Rollánd should die,</span>
<span class="i0">Would strike off Carle's right arm. Then on the field</span>
<span class="i0">That wond'rous host in death shall lie. No more</span>
<span class="i0">Thereafter could King Carle such forces raise,</span>
<span class="i0">And the Great Land at last would rest in peace."</span>
<span class="i0">Marsile, this hearing, kissed him on the neck,</span>
<span class="i0">And then began his treasures to display.</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span>
</div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_47">XLVII.</a></h4>
<div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">Exclaimed Marsile:—"What further [shall I say?]</span>
<span class="i0">No good adviser he of faith unsure.</span>
<span class="pagenum">[33]</span><span class="i0">Swear if Rollánd be there that he shall die!"</span>
<span class="i0">Thus answered Ganelon:—"Your will be done."</span>
<span class="i0">Upon the relics of his sword Murgleis</span>
<span class="i0">The treason swore; thus forfeited himself.</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span>
</div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_48">XLVIII.</a></h4>
<div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">An ivory-faldstool there was set. Marsile</span>
<span class="i0">The order gives to bring a book before it,</span>
<span class="i0">Mohammed's law and that of Tervagant,</span>
<span class="i0">The Spanish Saracen thus took his oath:</span>
<span class="i0">"If in the rear-guard Count Rollánd be found,</span>
<span class="i0">He will attack him there with all his men;</span>
<span class="i0">And, if it may be, there Rollánd shall die."</span>
<span class="i0">Ganelon answers:—"May [our treaty thrive!]"</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span>
</div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_49">XLIX.</a></h4>
<div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">Behold a Pagan, Valdabrun, who armed</span>
<span class="i0">Marsile a Knight; with cheerful smile he said</span>
<span class="i0">To Ganelon:—"Take this my sword; no man</span>
<span class="i0">E'er drew its peer; the hilt alone is worth</span>
<span class="i0">More than a thousand marks.—For love I give it,</span>
<span class="i0">But lend us help against the Count Rollánd,</span>
<span class="i0">And show us how to find him in the rear."</span>
<span class="i0">"—So shall it be," replies Count Ganelon;</span>
<span class="i0">Whereon they kissed each other's chin and face.</span>
</div></div>
<p><span class="pagenum">[34]</span></p>
<h4> <a id="St_50">L.</a></h4>
<div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">Another Pagan came. 'Twas Climorin</span>
<span class="i0">Who gayly smiling, said to Ganelon:</span>
<span class="i0">"My helmet take—None better have I seen,</span>
<span class="i0">But help us now against <i>Marchis</i> Rollánd</span>
<span class="i0">That we may throw dishonor on his name."</span>
<span class="i0">"—Well shall it be," responded Ganelon,</span>
<span class="i0">And then they kissed each other's lips and cheek.</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span>
</div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_51">LI.</a></h4><div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">And now behold, comes Bramimunde the Queen;</span>
<span class="i0">"Sire Ganelon," said she, "I love you much,</span>
<span class="i0">You, by my sire and all our men esteemed.</span>
<span class="i0">Two necklaces unto your wife I send,</span>
<span class="i0">With jacinths and with amethysts and gold</span>
<span class="i0">Adorned, worth more than all the wealth of Rome;</span>
<span class="i0">Jewels so rich your Emp'ror never had."</span>
<span class="i0">The Count receives and puts them in his hose.</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span></div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_52">LII.</a></h4><div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">The King calls up Malduit, his treasurer:</span>
<span class="i0">"Hast thou prepared my gifts for Carle the King?"</span>
<span class="pagenum">[35]</span><span class="i0">Malduit responds:—"Yea sire, the whole are there:</span>
<span class="i0">Seven hundred camels with their loads of gold</span>
<span class="i0">And silver; then of hostages a score,</span>
<span class="i0">The noblest ever lived beneath the stars."</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span></div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_53">LIII.</a></h4><div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">Marsile took by the shoulder Ganelon</span>
<span class="i0">And told him:—"Thou hast wisdom and art brave.</span>
<span class="i0">By that great law ye hold the best, beware</span>
<span class="i0">Thy heart fails not. Rich treasures will I give</span>
<span class="i0">To thee: ten mules laden with purest gold</span>
<span class="i0">From Araby; each year shall bring the like.</span>
<span class="i0">Meantime of this great city take the keys,</span>
<span class="i0">And in my name present this wealth to Carle.</span>
<span class="i0">But let Rollánd be ordered to the rear.</span>
<span class="i0">If in the pass or mount I find the knight,</span>
<span class="i0">I swear to give him combat to the death."</span>
<span class="i0">Says Ganelon:—"Methinks too long I stay."—</span>
<span class="i0">He mounts his horse and goes upon his way.</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span></div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_54">LIV.</a></h4><div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">The Emperor nears his realm, and reaching now</span>
<span class="i0">The city of Valterne sacked by Rollànd</span>
<span class="pagenum">[36]</span><span class="i0">And left in ruins, which thereafter lay</span>
<span class="i0">A hundred years a desert; there he waits</span>
<span class="i0">For news of Ganelon, and tribute due</span>
<span class="i0">By the great land of Spain. One morning when</span>
<span class="i0">The early dawn was brightening into day,</span>
<span class="i0">Count Ganelon drew nigh unto the camp.</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span></div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_55">LV.</a></h4><div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">In early morn the Emperor arose.</span>
<span class="i0">Having attended mass and matins both,</span>
<span class="i0">Upon the verdant grass, before his tent</span>
<span class="i0">He stood, surrounded by the Count Rollánd,</span>
<span class="i0">The valorous Olivier, and the Duke Naimes,</span>
<span class="i0">With many more besides. There also came</span>
<span class="i0">The perjurer, the treacherous Ganelon,</span>
<span class="i0">Who, stepping forth, with most perfidious tongue</span>
<span class="i0">Began to speak:—"Hail! God save Carle the King!—</span>
<span class="i0">I bring you here the keys of Sarraguce:</span>
<span class="i0">Great treasures follow through my care conveyed</span>
<span class="i0">With hostages a score. So, guard them well.</span>
<span class="i0">The King Marsile the brave bears not the blame</span>
<span class="i0">If I bring not the Kalif unto you.</span>
<span class="i0">Myself three hundred thousand men in arms</span>
<span class="i0">Beheld, with hauberks clad, and helmets clasped,</span>
<span class="pagenum">[37]</span><span class="i0">Swords by their sides, hilts bright with gold inlaid,</span>
<span class="i0">Who with him crossed the sea, not to submit</span>
<span class="i0">To Christ's law which they will not hold nor keep.</span>
<span class="i0">But scarce five leagues had they sailed on the main,</span>
<span class="i0">When wind and tempest rising, down they sank.</span>
<span class="i0">All perished!... Never shall you see them more.</span>
<span class="i0">Had but the Kalif lived, I would have brought</span>
<span class="i0">Him hither. For the Pagan King, know well,</span>
<span class="i0">Ere you shall see this first month pass away,</span>
<span class="i0">Your vassal will he be, with joinèd hands,</span>
<span class="i0">And hold the realm of Spain a fief from you."</span>
<span class="i0">Thus said the King:—"Thanks be to God for this!</span>
<span class="i0">Well have you done, and great your recompense</span>
<span class="i0">Shall be."—He bids a thousand trumpets sound...</span>
<span class="i0">The camp is struck:—the Franks then load their mules</span>
<span class="i0">And set forth on their journey to Sweet France.</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span></div></div>
<hr style="width: 50%;">
<p><span class="pagenum">[38]</span></p>
<h3>THE REAR GUARD.</h3>
<hr class="two">
<h4>ROLLÁND DOOMED TO DEATH.</h4>
<hr class="two">
<h4> <a id="St_56">LVI.</a></h4><div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">King Carle the Great has made a waste of Spain,</span>
<span class="i0">The cities violated, the castles seized.</span>
<span class="i0">Now saith the King his war is at an end,</span>
<span class="i0">And toward Sweet France the Emperor directs</span>
<span class="i0">His steed.... The Count Rollánd the pennon white</span>
<span class="i0">Has planted on a hill, high 'gainst the sky.</span>
<span class="i0">In all the country round the Franks their tents</span>
<span class="i0">Are pitching, while the Pagans ride along</span>
<span class="i0">The mighty vales. In hauberk clad—their backs</span>
<span class="i0">In armor cased; with helmets clasped—sword girt</span>
<span class="i0">On thigh—shields on their necks—each lance in rest,</span>
<span class="i0">Within a thicket on the mount they halt.</span>
<span class="i0">Four hundred thousand men there wait the dawn.</span>
<span class="i0">The French yet know it not. Ah God! what woe!</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span></div></div>
<p><span class="pagenum">[39]</span></p>
<h4> <a id="St_57">LVII.</a></h4><div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">Passes the day; the shades of night have fallen.</span>
<span class="i0">Carle the great Emp'ror sleeps; and in a dream</span>
<span class="i0">He marches through the deep defiles of Sizre.</span>
<span class="i0">In his right hand his ashen spear he holds,</span>
<span class="i0">Which suddenly Count Ganelon has snatched</span>
<span class="i0">From him, and shook and brandished in such wise</span>
<span class="i0">That, breaking, high tow'rd Heav'n the splinters flew.</span>
<span class="i0">Carle sleeps—naught from his slumber can arouse him.</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span></div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_58">LVIII.</a></h4><div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">Another vision followed hereupon:</span>
<span class="i0">He is in France, in his <i>Chapelle</i>, at Aix.</span>
<span class="i0">A bear his right arm caught with such sharp fangs</span>
<span class="i0">[That from the bone the flesh is torn away.]</span>
<span class="i0">From toward Ardennes he saw a leopard come,</span>
<span class="i0">Which in his dream, made on him fierce attack;</span>
<span class="i0">But then a greyhound dashes from the hall</span>
<span class="pagenum">[40]</span><span class="i0">Unto Carle's rescue, swift of leap and bound;</span>
<span class="i0">First from the treach'rous bear the hound tears off</span>
<span class="i0">An ear, then with the leopard combat makes.</span>
<span class="i0">"See!" cry the French, "what battle fierce is here."</span>
<span class="i0">But they know not which of the two will win</span>
<span class="i0">The field—Carle still asleep naught can awake.</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span></div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_59">LIX.</a></h4>
<div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">Vanished the night, and the clear dawn appeared.</span>
<span class="i0">With noble mien the Emperor mounts his steed,</span>
<span class="i0">And 'mid the host one thousand trumpets sound:</span>
<span class="i0">"<i>Barons</i>," said Carle:—"You see those deep defiles</span>
<span class="i0">And narrow passes—judge who in the rear</span>
<span class="i0">Will take command." Said Ganelon:—"Rollánd,</span>
<span class="i0">My step-son, whom among your valiant knights</span>
<span class="i0">You prize the most." Carle hearing this, upon</span>
<span class="i0">Him sternly looked:—"Thou art the devil's self,"</span>
<span class="i0">Said he, "or else a mortal rage has stung</span>
<span class="i0">Thy heart! Say, who before me in the van</span>
<span class="i0">Will march? 'Twill be Ogier de Dannemarche!</span>
<span class="i0">You have no better Baron for the post."</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span></div></div>
<p><span class="pagenum">[41]</span></p>
<h4> <a id="St_60">LX.</a></h4>
<div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">When hears the Count Rollánd the lot has fallen</span>
<span class="i0">Upon himself, as loyal knight he speaks:—</span>
<span class="i0">"You, sire step-father, dear and well beloved</span>
<span class="i0">Must be, since you have named me for the rear;</span>
<span class="i0">Nor shall Carl'magne, the King of France, lose aught,</span>
<span class="i0">Nor palfrey, nor fleet steed, if knowledge true</span>
<span class="i0">I have, nor male nor female mule that man</span>
<span class="i0">Can ride, nor beast of burden, horse or ass,</span>
<span class="i0">Unreckoned for with these good swords of ours."</span>
<span class="i0">Said Ganelon:—"The truth you speak, I know."</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span></div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_61">LXI.</a></h4><div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">When hears Rollánd the rear shall be his lot,</span>
<span class="i0">To his step-father thus in wrath he speaks:—</span>
<span class="i0">"Ah! traitor, evil man of race impure,</span>
<span class="i0">Thou thought'st to see me here let fall the glove</span>
<span class="i0">As thou erst dropped the staff before the King!"</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span></div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_62">LXII.</a></h4><div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">The Count Rollánd [addressing thus Carl'magne:]</span>
<span class="i0">"Give me the bow that now your hand doth hold,</span>
<span class="pagenum">[42]</span><span class="i0">For, to my knowledge, none will e'er throw blame</span>
<span class="i0">On me for dropping it, as fell on earth</span>
<span class="i0">Your right hand glove, when he received the staff."</span>
<span class="i0">With head declined the Emperor remains:</span>
<span class="i0">Oft plucks and twists the beard on lip and cheek,</span>
<span class="i0">Nor can his eyes restrain their falling tears.</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span></div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_63">LXIII.</a></h4><div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">Naimes after came—no better ever was</span>
<span class="i0">A vassal in the court. He said to Carle:</span>
<span class="i0">"You hear him; greatly wroth is Count Rollánd;</span>
<span class="i0">The rear guard is assigned to his command;</span>
<span class="i0">No baron have you that with him would make</span>
<span class="i0">Exchange. Give him the bow and your hand has bent,</span>
<span class="i0">And look for those who best may lend him help."</span>
<span class="i0">Carle gives the bow which Count Rollánd receives.</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span></div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_64">LXIV.</a></h4><div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">The Emperor calls to Rollánd and says:—</span>
<span class="i0">"Fair sire, my nephew, truly you must know</span>
<span class="i0">Half of my army will I leave with you;</span>
<span class="pagenum">[43]</span><span class="i0">Keep them; in their good help your safety lies."</span>
<span class="i0">Then said the Count:—"Of this will I do naught!</span>
<span class="i0">May God confound me, ere my race I shame;</span>
<span class="i0">But twenty thousand valiant knights I keep!</span>
<span class="i0">Through the defiles you can in safety pass</span>
<span class="i0">And fear no harm from man while yet I live."</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span></div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_65">LXV.</a></h4><div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">Rollánd sits on his steed, and nigh him rides</span>
<span class="i0">His comrade Olivier. There came Gerin,</span>
<span class="i0">Gerier the brave, Othon and Berengier;</span>
<span class="i0">There came Sansun, Anseïs the fierce; there came</span>
<span class="i0">Also Gerard de Roussillon the old,</span>
<span class="i0">Together with the <i>Gascuin</i> Engelier.</span>
<span class="i0">The Archbishop said:—"I, by my head, will go!"</span>
<span class="i0">"—And I with you," exclaimed the Count Gualtier;</span>
<span class="i0">"Rollánd's own man am I, and follow him!"</span>
<span class="i0">From all are chosen twenty thousand knights.</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span></div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_66">LXVI.</a></h4><div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">The Count Rollánd calls up Gualtier de l'Hum:</span>
<span class="i0">"One thousand Franks of France, our land, array,</span>
<span class="i0">And with them cover heights and passes, that</span>
<span class="pagenum">[44]</span><span class="i0">The Emperor may lose none of his host."</span>
<span class="i0">Responds Gualtier:—"This am I bound to do</span>
<span class="i0">For you."—Forthwith one thousand Franks of France</span>
<span class="i0">O'errun each height and pass.—None shall descend</span>
<span class="i0">Despite ill news, ere seven hundred swords</span>
<span class="i0">Unsheathe. That very day King Almaris</span>
<span class="i0">Who rules Belferne, met them with battle fierce.</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span></div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_67">LXVII.</a></h4><div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">High are the mounts, the valleys murky-dark—</span>
<span class="i0">The rocks are black, the gorges terrible.</span>
<span class="i0">The French toiled through them painfully; their march</span>
<span class="i0">Was heard for fifteen leagues; then the Great land</span>
<span class="i0">Reaching, they viewed Gascuigne, their lord's estate,</span>
<span class="i0">And sweet remembrance felt of honors, fiefs,</span>
<span class="i0">Of lovely maidens and of noble wives:</span>
<span class="i0">Not one is there but weeps from tenderness;</span>
<span class="i0">But more than all is Carle distressed; he mourns</span>
<span class="i0">His nephew left in the defiles of Spain....</span>
<span class="i0">By pity moved he cannot choose but weep.</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span></div></div>
<p><span class="pagenum">[45]</span></p>
<h4> <a id="St_68">LXVIII.</a></h4><div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">The twelve Peers staid in Spain. A thousand score</span>
<span class="i0">Of Franks are under their command, to whom</span>
<span class="i0">Unknown is wavering fear or dread of death.</span>
<span class="i0">Carl'magne to France returns—within his cloak</span>
<span class="i0">He hides his face—Naimes, riding near, inquired:</span>
<span class="i0">"What thought, O King, weighs now upon your heart?"—</span>
<span class="i0">"Who questions me doth wrong. So sad am I</span>
<span class="i0">I can but mourn. Sweet France by Ganelon</span>
<span class="i0">Shall be destroyed. An angel in my sleep</span>
<span class="i0">Appeared, and, dreaming, I beheld my lance</span>
<span class="i0">Broken up within my hand by him who named</span>
<span class="i0">My nephew for the rear guard ... and I left</span>
<span class="i0">Him in a foreign land;—O mighty God,</span>
<span class="i0">Should I lose him, I ne'er should find his peer!"</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span></div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_69">LXIX.</a></h4><div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">Carle the great King, no more restrains his tears:</span>
<span class="i0">One hundred thousand Franks great sympathy</span>
<span class="i0">Give him, with strangest fear for Count Rollánd.</span>
<span class="i0">Vile Ganelon, the wretch, this treason wrought!</span>
<span class="pagenum">[46]</span><span class="i0">He, from the Pagan King received rich gifts</span>
<span class="i0">Of gold and silver, silk and ciclatons,</span>
<span class="i0">Lions and camels, horses, mules. Behold,</span>
<span class="i0">King Marsile summons all his Counts from Spain,</span>
<span class="i0">His Viscounts, Dukes and Almazours; with these</span>
<span class="i0">The Emirs, and the sons of noble Counts;</span>
<span class="i0">Four hundred thousand gathered in three days,</span>
<span class="i0">In Sarraguce are beaten all the drums;</span>
<span class="i0">Mohammed's image to the loftiest tower</span>
<span class="i0">Is raised on high.—No Pagan but adores</span>
<span class="i0">And prays before him.—They then madly ride</span>
<span class="i0">Throughout the land, o'er mountain and o'er vale.</span>
<span class="i0">At last they see the gonfalons of France;</span>
<span class="i0">It is the rear-guard of the twelve compeers:</span>
<span class="i0">Nor will they fail to give them battle now.</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span></div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_70">LXX.</a></h4>
<div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">Hastes to the front the nephew of Marsile,</span>
<span class="i0">Goading the mule that bears him, with a staff.</span>
<span class="i0">Says to his uncle, gayly laughing loud:</span>
<span class="i0">"Fair King, till now I served you well; for you</span>
<span class="i0">Endured hard pain and grief.—The only fee</span>
<span class="i0">I ask is this:—To strike Rollánd! I swear</span>
<span class="i0">To give him death with my good trenchant sword,</span>
<span class="pagenum">[47]</span><span class="i0">And if his help Mohammed will bestow,</span>
<span class="i0">On me, forever shall all Spain be free,</span>
<span class="i0">From the defiles of Aspre to Durestant.</span>
<span class="i0">Carle then will yield,—the Franks, surrender all;</span>
<span class="i0">No more in all your life will you have war."</span>
<span class="i0">The King Marsile bestows on him the glove.</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span></div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_71">LXXI.</a></h4><div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">The nephew of Marsile holds in his grasp</span>
<span class="i0">The glove, and to the King with haughty pride</span>
<span class="i0">Speaks:—"Fair Sire King, your gift I dearly prize;</span>
<span class="i0">Choose you for me eleven of your Knights,</span>
<span class="i0">And I will go and combat the twelve Peers."</span>
<span class="i0">The first response was that of Falsaun:</span>
<span class="i0">He was the brother of the King Marsile.—</span>
<span class="i0">"Fair nephew, we shall go, both you and I;</span>
<span class="i0">In battle side by side, we shall engage.</span>
<span class="i0">The rear of Carle's great host is doomed to die!"</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span></div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_72">LXXII.</a></h4><div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">King Corsalis stands on the other side;</span>
<span class="i0">He comes from Barbary; a soul of guile.</span>
<span class="i0">Still speaks he there not unlike vassal true</span>
<span class="i0">Who would not for the gold of heav'n be base:</span>
<span class="pagenum">[48]</span><span class="i0">"If there I find Rollànd, we meet in fight.</span>
<span class="i0">I am the third; now choose ye out the fourth."</span>
<span class="i0">See you the spurring Malprimis de Brigal,</span>
<span class="i0">Faster on foot than runs the fastest steed?</span>
<span class="i0">Before Marsile in a loud voice he cries:</span>
<span class="i0">"I shall my body take to Ronceval;</span>
<span class="i0">If there I find Rollànd, by me he dies."</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span></div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_73">LXXIII.</a></h4><div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">An Emir now is there, from Balaguer.</span>
<span class="i0">Of handsome form, with proud and cheerful face,</span>
<span class="i0">When on his steed he vaults, well doth he show</span>
<span class="i0">With what great pride his armor's mail is borne.</span>
<span class="i0">For truest vassalage he is renowned;</span>
<span class="i0">Were he but Christian, 'twere a baron true.</span>
<span class="i0">Before Marsile he stands and loudly cries:</span>
<span class="i0">"My body I will take to Ronceval;</span>
<span class="i0">If there I face Rollànd his doom is sure,</span>
<span class="i0">Eke Olivier and the twelve peers, all die.</span>
<span class="i0">The Franks shall perish in despair and shame.</span>
<span class="i0">Carl'magne is old and dotes. O'erwhelmed, at last</span>
<span class="i0">He will give up this waging war, and Spain</span>
<span class="i0">Forever shall be kept beneath our sway."</span>
<span class="i0">The King Marsile on him bestows great thanks.</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span></div></div>
<p><span class="pagenum">[49]</span></p>
<h4> <a id="St_74">LXXIV.</a></h4><div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">Then from the Moorish land an Almazour</span>
<span class="i0">Steps forth. All Spain can show no greater wretch.</span>
<span class="i0">Before Marsile he makes a boastful vaunt:</span>
<span class="i0">"To Ronceval will I my people lead—</span>
<span class="i0">Full twenty thousand men with lance and shield.</span>
<span class="i0">If I Rollànd find there, I pledge his death;</span>
<span class="i0">No after-day shall dawn but Carle shall weep."</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span></div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_75">LXXV.</a></h4><div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">From elsewhere comes Turgis de Turteluse.</span>
<span class="i0">He is a count, and o'er this city wields</span>
<span class="i0">His sway; hate unto Christians has he vowed,</span>
<span class="i0">And stands with all the rest before Marsile.</span>
<span class="i0">He thus addressed the king: "Ne'er be dismayed!</span>
<span class="i0">More worth Mohammed than Saint Pierre of Rome;</span>
<span class="i0">But serve him well, the honor of the field</span>
<span class="i0">[Is ours]. I'll meet Rollánd at Ronceval</span>
<span class="i0">Where none can guard him. Mark this sword of mine;</span>
<span class="i0">Its blade, so good and long, in desperate fight</span>
<span class="i0">Will cross with Durendal; and you will hear</span>
<span class="i0">Which of the two shall win the victory.</span>
<span class="pagenum">[50]</span><span class="i0">Abandoned unto us the French must die.</span>
<span class="i0">The old King Carle will have both grief and shame,</span>
<span class="i0">And never more on earth will wear a crown."</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span></div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_76">LXXVI.</a></h4><div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">Comes up besides Escremiz de Valterne,</span>
<span class="i0">A Saracen, and of that country lord.</span>
<span class="i0">Before Marsile among the throng he cries:</span>
<span class="i0">"To Ronceval I go, to crush the proud;</span>
<span class="i0">Nor shall Rollánd, if there, bear off his head,</span>
<span class="i0">Nor Olivier, chief of the other knights;</span>
<span class="i0">The twelve peers, all are doomed to perish there.</span>
