<span class="i0">Untouched. Rollánd at this, upon him looks,</span>
<span class="i0">And softly, sweetly asks:—"Sire <i>compagnon</i>!</span>
<span class="i0">Was that blow meant for me? I am Rollánd</span>
<span class="i0">By whom you are beloved so well; to me</span>
<span class="i0">Could you by any chance, defiance give?"</span>
<span class="i0">Said Olivier:—"I hear your speech, but see</span>
<span class="i0">You now no more. May God behold you, friend!</span>
<span class="i0">I struck the blow; beseech you, pardon me."</span>
<span class="pagenum">[106]</span><span class="i0">Rollánd responds:—"I am not wounded—here</span>
<span class="i0">And before God I pardon you." At this,</span>
<span class="i0">Each to the other bends in courtesy.</span>
<span class="i0">With such great tenderness and love they part.</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span></div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_152">CLII.</a></h4><div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">Olivier feels the agony of death;</span>
<span class="i0">His vacant eyes roll wildly in his head,</span>
<span class="i0">And all his hearing and his sight are lost.</span>
<span class="i0">Dismounting, on the ground he lies, and smites</span>
<span class="i0">His breast, aloud confessing all his sins;</span>
<span class="i0">With joined hands tow'rd Heaven lifted up</span>
<span class="i0">He prays to God to give him Paradise,</span>
<span class="i0">To bless Carl'magne, sweet France, and far beyond</span>
<span class="i0">All other men, Rollánd, his <i>compagnon</i>.</span>
<span class="i0">His heart fails—forward droops his helmet—prone</span>
<span class="i0">Upon the earth he lies—'tis over now....</span>
<span class="i0">The Count is dead. Rollánd, the Baron, mourns</span>
<span class="i0">And weeps as never mortal mourned before.</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span></div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_153">CLIII.</a></h4><div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">When sees the Count Rollánd the breath of life</span>
<span class="i0">Gone from his friend, his body stretched on earth,</span>
<span class="pagenum">[107]</span><span class="i0">His face low in the dust, his tears gush out</span>
<span class="i0">With heavy sobs. Then tenderly he speaks:</span>
<span class="i0">"Alas! for all thy valor, comrade dear!</span>
<span class="i0">Year after year, day after day, a life</span>
<span class="i0">Of love we led; ne'er didst thou wrong to me,</span>
<span class="i0">Nor I to thee. If death takes thee away,</span>
<span class="i0">My life is but a pain." While speaking thus,</span>
<span class="i0">The <i>Marchis</i> faints on Veillantif, his steed.</span>
<span class="i0">But still firm in his stirrups of pure gold:</span>
<span class="i0">Where'er Rollánd may ride, he cannot fall.</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span></div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_154">CLIV.</a></h4><div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">Scarce hath the Count recovered from his swoon,</span>
<span class="i0">When all the great disaster meets his sight;</span>
<span class="i0">The French lie on the field; all lost to him</span>
<span class="i0">Save the Archbishop and Gualtier de l'Hum,</span>
<span class="i0">Who had descended from the mountain height</span>
<span class="i0">Where he the men of Spain all day withstood</span>
<span class="i0">Till all his own fell 'neath the Pagan swords.</span>
<span class="i0">Willed he or not, he fled into the vale,</span>
<span class="i0">And now upon Rollánd he calls for aid;</span>
<span class="i0">"Most gentle Count, most valiant, where art thou?</span>
<span class="i0">Ne'er had I fear where'er thou wert!—'tis I,</span>
<span class="pagenum">[108]</span><span class="i0">Gualtier, who conquered Maëlgut, who am</span>
<span class="i0">Old gray-haired Droün's nephew; till this day</span>
<span class="i0">My courage won thy love. So well I fought</span>
<span class="i0">Against the Saracens, my spear was broke,</span>
<span class="i0">My shield was pierced, my hauberk torn and wrung,</span>
<span class="i0">And in my body eight steel darts I bear.</span>
<span class="i0">Done are my days, but dear the last I sold!"</span>
<span class="i0">The words of that brave knight Rollánd has heard,</span>
<span class="i0">Spurs on his steed and gallops to his help.</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span></div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_155">CLV.</a></h4><div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">With grief and rage Rollánd's great heart is full;</span>
<span class="i0">Amidst the thick ranks of a swarming foe</span>
<span class="i0">He rides. He fights—and twenty Pagans fall</span>
<span class="i0">Slain by his hand; by Gualtier's six, and five</span>
<span class="i0">By the Archbishop's. Loud the Pagans cry:</span>
<span class="i0">"Vile wretches these! Let none escape alive!</span>
<span class="i0">Eternal shame to them who dare not make</span>
<span class="i0">Attack; foul recreants those who let their flight</span>
<span class="i0">Avail."—Renewing then their hues and cries,</span>
<span class="i0">The Pagans rush from all parts 'gainst the knights.</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span></div></div>
<hr style="width: 50%;">
<p><span class="pagenum">[109]</span></p>
<h4>CHARLEMAGNE APPROACHES.</h4>
<h4> <a id="St_156">CLVI.</a></h4><div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">The Count Rollánd was ever great in war;</span>
<span class="i0">Most valiant is Gualtier de l'Hum; Turpin</span>
<span class="i0">The Archbishop, of a valor proved: each leaves</span>
<span class="i0">The other naught to do, and 'mid the throng</span>
<span class="i0">Strikes Pagans down, who though one thousand foot</span>
<span class="i0">And forty thousand horsemen mustering, yet</span>
<span class="i0">Dare not approach, forsooth; but from afar</span>
<span class="i0">Against them hurl their jav'lins, spears and darts,</span>
<span class="i0">Their lances and winged arrows. First of all</span>
<span class="i0">Is slain Gualtier; Turpin de Reins' good shield</span>
<span class="i0">Is pierced, his helmet broken, and his head</span>
<span class="i0">Wounded, his hauberk shattered and dislinked;</span>
<span class="i0">Four spears have pierced his body; his good steed</span>
<span class="i0">Dies under him. Alas! the Archbishop falls.</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span></div></div>
<p><span class="pagenum">[110]</span></p>
<h4> <a id="St_157">CLVII.</a></h4><div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">Hardly had Turpin fallen on the earth,</span>
<span class="i0">By four spear-shafts transfixed, when the brave knight</span>
<span class="i0">Sprang quickly to his feet once more. His look</span>
<span class="i0">Sought for Rollánd to whom he ran in haste.</span>
<span class="i0">One word he said:—"Unconquered yet am I!</span>
<span class="i0">While life doth last, a true knight yields it not!"</span>
<span class="i0">He draws Almace, his sword of burnished steel,</span>
<span class="i0">And rushing 'mid the throng, one thousand blows</span>
<span class="i0">And more he deals.—Carle said in after days,</span>
<span class="i0">Turpin spared none, as dead upon the field</span>
<span class="i0">He saw four hundred men, some cut in twain,</span>
<span class="i0">Some with lopped heads: so says the Geste of France,</span>
<span class="i0">And one who saw the field, the brave Saint-Gille</span>
<span class="i0">For whom God showed his might; who in the cloister</span>
<span class="i0">Of Loüm wrote the record of these deeds.</span>
<span class="i0">Who knows not this, he knows not any thing.</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span></div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_158">CLVIII.</a></h4><div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">As hero fights the Count Rollánd; but all</span>
<span class="i0">His body burns with heat and drips with sweat;</span>
<span class="pagenum">[111]</span><span class="i0">His head is torn by pain; his temple burst</span>
<span class="i0">By that strong blast he gave the olifant.</span>
<span class="i0">Still would he know if Carle returns; once more</span>
<span class="i0">He blows his horn—Alas, with feeble blast.</span>
<span class="i0">Carle caught the distant sound, and, list'ning, waits:</span>
<span class="i0">"Seigneurs," cried he, "great evils fall apace;</span>
<span class="i0">I hear his dying blast upon his horn.</span>
<span class="i0">If we would find him yet alive, we need</span>
<span class="i0">Urge on our steeds. Let all our trumpets blow!"</span>
<span class="i0">Then sixty thousand trumps rang forth their peals;</span>
<span class="i0">The hills reëcho, and the vales respond.</span>
<span class="i0">The Pagans hear—and stay their gabbling mirth.</span>
<span class="i0">One to the other says:—"'Tis Carle who comes!"</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span></div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_159">CLIX.</a></h4><div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">The Pagans say:—"The Emperor returns;</span>
<span class="i0">These are the clarions of the French we hear.</span>
<span class="i0">If Carle should come, 'twill be our doom; if lives</span>
<span class="i0">Rollánd, the war begins anew, and Spain</span>
<span class="i0">Our land is lost to us for evermore."</span>
<span class="i0">Four hundred warriors well armed cap-a-pie,</span>
<span class="i0">The bravest of the host, then closed their ranks</span>
<span class="i0">And dashed in fierce attack against Rollánd.</span>
<span class="i0">Mighty the deeds the Count must now achieve!</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span></div></div>
<p><span class="pagenum">[112]</span></p>
<h4> <a id="St_160">CLX.</a></h4><div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">As they draw near, Rollánd calls up his pride</span>
<span class="i0">And summons all his strength to meet the charge.</span>
<span class="i0">No foot of ground he yields while life remains.</span>
<span class="i0">Firm on his courser Veillantif he sits</span>
<span class="i0">And gores his flanks with spurs of purest gold.</span>
<span class="i0">Into the thickest ranks he and Turpin</span>
<span class="i0">The Archbishop rush. And now the Pagans all</span>
<span class="i0">Unto each other cry: "Hence, friends, away!</span>
<span class="i0">The horns of those of France we now have heard,</span>
<span class="i0">Carlemagne the mighty Emperor returns!"</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span></div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_161">CLXI.</a></h4><div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">Ne'er could the Count Rollánd a coward love,</span>
<span class="i0">Nor proud, nor wicked men, nor faithless knights.</span>
<span class="i0">He calls to the Archbishop: "You, on foot,</span>
<span class="i0">And I on horseback, sire! For love of you</span>
<span class="i0">I by your side will stand; together we</span>
<span class="i0">Will share or good or ill; I leave you not</span>
<span class="i0">For aught of human mold. This day we shall</span>
<span class="i0">Hurl back the Pagan charge, and Durendal</span>
<span class="pagenum">[113]</span><span class="i0">Shall deal his mightiest blows!"—To this replies</span>
<span class="i0">The Archbishop: "Traitor he who strikes not well!</span>
<span class="i0">King Carle returns—Great shall his vengeance be!"</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span></div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_162">CLXII.</a></h4><div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">The Pagans say: "For such ill were we born!</span>
<span class="i0">What fatal morn this day for us has ris'n!</span>
<span class="i0">Dead lie our lords and Peers! With his great host</span>
<span class="i0">King Carle returns, the mighty Baron—Hark!</span>
<span class="i0">His clarions sound, and loud the cry 'Montjoie;'</span>
<span class="i0">Rollánd has so great pride, no man of flesh</span>
<span class="i0">Can make him yield, or vanquished fall. 'Twere best</span>
<span class="i0">We pierced him from afar, and left him lying</span>
<span class="i0">Upon the field!"——'Twas done: darts, lances, spears,</span>
<span class="i0">Javelins, winged arrows flew so thick,</span>
<span class="i0">That his good shield was pierced, his hauberk rent</span>
<span class="i0">And torn apart—his body yet unharmed.</span>
<span class="i0">Veillantif, pierced with thirty wounds, falls dead</span>
<span class="i0">Beneath the Count.—The affrighted Pagans fly.</span>
<span class="i0">The Count Rollánd stands on the field, alone.</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span></div></div>
<hr style="width: 50%;">
<p><span class="pagenum">[114]</span></p>
<h4>THE LAST BENEDICTION OF THE ARCHBISHOP.</h4>
<h4> <a id="St_163">CLXIII.</a></h4><div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">Raging in wrath the Pagans fly, and toward</span>
<span class="i0">The land of Spain they haste. The Count Rollánd</span>
<span class="i0">Pursues them not, for Veillantif lies dead.</span>
<span class="i0">On foot he stands whether he will or not.</span>
<span class="i0">To help Turpin, the Archbishop, fast he ran,</span>
<span class="i0">His helm unclasped, removed the hauberk white</span>
<span class="i0">And light, then ripped the sides of his <i>blialt</i></span>
<span class="i0">To find his gaping wounds; then tenderly</span>
<span class="i0">Pressing him in his arms, on the green sward</span>
<span class="i0">He laid him gently down, and fondly prayed:</span>
<span class="i0">"O noble man, grant me your leave in this;</span>
<span class="i0">Our brave compeers, so dear to us, have breathed</span>
<span class="i0">Their last—we should not leave them on the field;</span>
<span class="i0">I will their bodies seek and gather here,</span>
<span class="i0">To lay them out before you."—"Go, and soon</span>
<span class="i0">Return," the Archbishop said; "the field is yours</span>
<span class="i0">And also mine, thanks to Almighty God!"</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span></div></div>
<p><span class="pagenum">[115]</span></p>
<h4> <a id="St_164">CLXIV.</a></h4><div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">Alone the Count Rollánd retraced his steps</span>
<span class="i0">Throughout the field. Vales, mounts, he searched, and found</span>
<span class="i0">Gerin and his companion Gerier, then</span>
<span class="i0">Berengier and Otun; here Anseïs,</span>
<span class="i0">There Sansun, then beyond, Gerard the old</span>
<span class="i0">De Roussillon he found—one after one</span>
<span class="i0">He bore each knight within his arms, and placed</span>
<span class="i0">Them gently, side by side, before the knees</span>
<span class="i0">Of Turpin who cannot restrain his tears;</span>
<span class="i0">With lifted hands he blesses them and says:</span>
<span class="i0">"Most hapless Knights!—May God the Glorious</span>
<span class="i0">Receive your souls, and in his Paradise</span>
<span class="i0">'Mid holy flowers place them!—In this hour</span>
<span class="i0">Of death, my deepest grief is that no more</span>
<span class="i0">The mighty Emperor I shall behold!"</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span></div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_165">CLXV.</a></h4><div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">Rollánd turns back, and searching through the field,</span>
<span class="i0">Has found, alas! his comrade Olivier....</span>
<span class="i0">He pressed him 'gainst his bosom tenderly,</span>
<span class="i0">And, as he could, returning to Turpin,</span>
<span class="i0">Stretched on a shield he lays him down among</span>
<span class="pagenum">[116]</span><span class="i0">The other knights. The Archbishop then assoils</span>
<span class="i0">And signs him with the holy cross. The grief</span>
<span class="i0">And pity were more sore than heart can bear....</span>
<span class="i0">Then said Rollánd:—"Fair comrade Olivier,</span>
<span class="i0">Son of the good Count Renier, he who held</span>
<span class="i0">The marches to the distant shores of Gennes;</span>
<span class="i0">To break a lance, to pierce a shield, the brave</span>
<span class="i0">To counsel, traitors to dismay and foil,</span>
<span class="i0">No land e'er saw a better <i>chevalier</i>."</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span></div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_166">CLXVI.</a></h4><div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">When Count Rollánd beheld his Peers lie dead,</span>
<span class="i0">And Olivier, that friend so tenderly</span>
<span class="i0">Beloved, his soul by pity was o'erflowed;</span>
<span class="i0">Tears from his eyes gush out, his countenance</span>
<span class="i0">Turns pale; distressed, he can no longer stand.</span>
<span class="i0">Would he or not, he swooned and fell to earth.</span>
<span class="i0">The Archbishop said: "Baron, what woe is yours!"</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span></div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_167">CLXVII.</a></h4><div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">The Archbishop, when he saw Count Rollánd swoon,</span>
<span class="i0">Felt keener grief than e'er he felt before;</span>
<span class="pagenum">[117]</span><span class="i0">Stretched forth his hand, and took the olifant.—</span>
<span class="i0">Ronceval there is a running stream;</span>
<span class="i0">Thence will he water bring to Count Rollánd.</span>
<span class="i0">Staggering, with feeble steps, thither he goes,</span>
<span class="i0">But loss of blood has made him all too weak:</span>
<span class="i0">Ere he has gone an acre's length, his heart</span>
<span class="i0">Fails, and he sinks in mortal agony.</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span></div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_168">CLXVIII.</a></h4><div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">Meantime the Count Rollánd revives.—Erect</span>
<span class="i0">He stands, but with great pain; then downward looks</span>
<span class="i0">And upward. Then he sees the noble lord</span>
<span class="i0">The Archbishop, holy minister of God,</span>
<span class="i0">Beyond his comrades lying on the sward</span>
<span class="i0">Stretched out.—He lifts his eyes to Heav'n, recalls</span>
<span class="i0">His sins, and raising both his joinèd hands,</span>
<span class="i0">He prays Our God to grant him paradise.—</span>
<span class="i0">Turpin, Carle's Knight, is dead, who all his life,</span>
<span class="i0">With doughty blows and sermons erudite,</span>
<span class="i0">Ne'er ceased to fight the Pagans. May the Lord</span>
<span class="i0">Grant him His holy blessing evermore!</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span></div></div>
<p><span class="pagenum">[118]</span></p>
<h4> <a id="St_169">CLXIX.</a></h4><div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">The Count Rollánd sees lifeless on the field</span>
<span class="i0">The Archbishop lie; gush from the gaping wounds</span>
<span class="i0">His entrails in the dust, and through his skull</span>
<span class="i0">The oozing brain pours o'er his brow.—In form</span>
<span class="i0">Of holy Cross upon his breast Rollánd</span>
<span class="i0">Disposes both his hands so fair and white,</span>
<span class="i0">And mourned him in the fashion of his land:</span>
<span class="i0">"O noble man! O knight of lineage pure!</span>
<span class="i0">To the Glorious One of Heav'n I thee commend;</span>
<span class="i0">For ne'er was man who Him more truly served,</span>
<span class="i0">Nor since the Apostles' days, such prophet, strong,</span>
<span class="i0">To keep God's law and draw the hearts of men.</span>
<span class="i0">From ev'ry pain your soul be freed, and wide</span>
<span class="i0">Before it ope the Gates of Paradise!"</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span></div></div>
<hr style="width: 50%;">
<p><span class="pagenum">[119]</span></p>
<h4>ROLAND'S DEATH.</h4>
<h4> <a id="St_170">CLXX.</a></h4><div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">Rollánd now feels his death is drawing nigh:</span>
<span class="i0">From both his ears the brain is oozing fast.</span>
<span class="i0">For all his peers he prays that God may call</span>
<span class="i0">Their souls to Him; to the Angel Gabriel</span>
<span class="i0">He recommends his spirit. In one hand</span>
<span class="i0">He takes the olifant, that no reproach</span>
<span class="i0">May rest upon him; in the other grasps</span>
<span class="i0">Durendal, his good sword. Forward he goes,</span>
<span class="i0">Far as an arblast sends a shaft, across</span>
<span class="i0">A new-tilled ground and toward the land of Spain.</span>
<span class="i0">Upon a hill, beneath two lofty trees,</span>
<span class="i0">Four terraces of marble spread:—he falls</span>
<span class="i0">Prone fainting on the green, for death draws near.</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span></div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_171">CLXXI.</a></h4><div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">High are the mounts, and lofty are the trees.</span>
<span class="i0">Four terraces are there, of marble bright:</span>
<span class="pagenum">[120]</span><span class="i0">There Count Rollánd lies senseless on the grass.</span>
<span class="i0">Him at this moment spies a Saracen</span>
<span class="i0">Who lies among the corpses, feigning death,</span>
<span class="i0">His face and body all besmeared with blood.</span>
<span class="i0">Sudden he rises to his feet, and bounds</span>
<span class="i0">Upon the Baron.—Handsome, brave and strong</span>
<span class="i0">He was, but from his pride sprang mortal rage.</span>
<span class="i0">He seized the body of Rollánd, and grasped</span>
<span class="i0">His arms, exclaiming thus:—"Here vanquished Carle's</span>
<span class="i0">Great nephew lies!"—"This sword to Araby</span>
<span class="i0">I'll bear."—He drew it;—this aroused the Count.</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span></div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_172">CLXXII.</a></h4><div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">Rollánd perceived an alien hand would rob</span>
<span class="i0">Him of his sword; his eyes he oped; one word</span>
<span class="i0">He spoke:—"I trow, not one of us art thou!"</span>
<span class="i0">Then with his olifant from which he parts</span>
<span class="i0">Never, he smites the golden studded helm,</span>
<span class="i0">Crushing the steel, the head, the bones; both eyes</span>
<span class="i0">Are from their sockets beaten out—o'erthrown</span>
<span class="i0">Dead at the Baron's feet he falls:—"O wretch,"</span>
<span class="i0">He cries, "how durst thou, or for good or ill,</span>
<span class="pagenum">[121]</span>
<span class="i0">Lay hands upon Rollánd? Who hears of this</span>
<span class="i0">Will call thee fool. Mine olifant is cleft,</span>
<span class="i0">Its gems and gold all scattered by the blow."</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span></div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_173">CLXXIII.</a></h4><div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">Now feels Rollánd that death is near at hand</span>
<span class="i0">And struggles up with all his force; his face</span>
<span class="i0">Grows livid;—[Durendal, his naked sword]</span>
<span class="i0">He holds;—beside him rises a gray rock</span>
<span class="i0">On which he strikes ten mighty blows through grief</span>
<span class="i0">And rage—The steel but grinds; it breaks not, nor</span>
<span class="i0">Is notched; then cries the Count:—"Saint Mary, help!</span>
<span class="i0">O Durendal! Good sword! ill starred art thou!</span>
<span class="i0">Though we two part, I care not less for thee.</span>
<span class="i0">What victories together thou and I,</span>
<span class="i0">Have gained, what kingdoms conquered, which now holds</span>
<span class="i0">White-bearded Carle! No coward's hand shall grasp</span>
<span class="i0">Thy hilt: a valiant knight has borne thee long,</span>
<span class="i0">Such as none shall e'er bear in France the Free!"</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span></div></div>
<p><span class="pagenum">[122]</span></p>
<h4> <a id="St_174">CLXXIV.</a></h4><div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">Rollánd smites hard the rock of Sardonix;</span>
<span class="i0">The steel but grinds, it breaks not, nor grows blunt;</span>
<span class="i0">Then seeing that he can not break his sword,</span>
<span class="i0">Thus to himself he mourns for Durendal:</span>
<span class="i0">"O good my sword, how bright and pure! Against</span>
<span class="i0">The sun what flashing light thy blade reflects!</span>
<span class="i0">When Carle passed through the valley of Moriane,</span>
<span class="i0">The God of Heaven by his Angel sent</span>
<span class="i0">Command that he should give thee to a Count,</span>
<span class="i0">A valiant captain; it was then the great</span>
<span class="i0">And gentle King did gird thee to my side.—</span>
<span class="i0">With thee I won for him Anjou—Bretaigne;</span>
<span class="i0">For him with thee I won Poitou, le Maine</span>
<span class="i0">And Normandie the free; I won Provence</span>
<span class="i0">And Aquitaine, and Lumbardie, and all</span>
<span class="i0">The Romanie; I won for him Bavière,</span>
<span class="i0">All Flandre—Buguerie—all Puillanie,</span>
<span class="i0">Costentinnoble which allegiance paid,</span>
<span class="i0">And Saxonie submitted to his power;</span>
<span class="i0">For him I won Escoce and Galle, Irlande</span>
<span class="i0">And Engleterre he made his royal seat;</span>
<span class="pagenum">[123]</span><span class="i0">With thee I conquered all the lands and realms</span>
<span class="i0">Which Carle, the hoary-bearded monarch, rules.</span>
<span class="i0">Now for this sword I mourn.... Far better die</span>
<span class="i0">Than in the hands of Pagans let it fall!</span>
<span class="i0">May God, Our Father, save sweet France this shame!"</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span></div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_175">CLXXV.</a></h4><div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">Upon the grey rock mightily he smites,</span>
<span class="i0">Shattering it more than I can tell; the sword</span>
<span class="i0">But grinds.—It breaks not—nor receives a notch,</span>
<span class="i0">And upwards springs more dazzling in the air.</span>
<span class="i0">When sees the Count Rollánd his sword can never break,</span>
<span class="i0">Softly within himself its fate he mourns:</span>
<span class="i0">"O Durendal, how fair and holy thou!</span>
<span class="i0">In thy gold-hilt are relics rare; a tooth</span>
<span class="i0">Of great saint Pierre—some blood of Saint Basile,</span>
<span class="i0">A lock of hair of Monseigneur Saint Denis,</span>
<span class="i0">A fragment of the robe of Sainte-Marie.</span>
<span class="i0">It is not right that Pagans should own thee;</span>
<span class="i0">By Christian hand alone be held. Vast realms</span>
<span class="pagenum">[124]</span>
<span class="i0">I shall have conquered once that now are ruled</span>
<span class="i0">By Carle, the King with beard all blossom-white,</span>
<span class="i0">And by them made great emperor and Lord.</span>
<span class="i0">May thou ne'er fall into a cowardly hand."</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span></div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_176">CLXXVI.</a></h4><div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">The Count Rollánd feels through his limbs the grasp</span>
<span class="i0">Of death, and from his head ev'n to his heart</span>
<span class="i0">A mortal chill descends. Unto a pine</span>
<span class="i0">He hastens, and falls stretched upon the grass.</span>
<span class="i0">Beneath him lie his sword and olifant,</span>
<span class="i0">And toward the Heathen land he turns his head,</span>
<span class="i0">That Carle and all his knightly host may say:</span>
<span class="i0">"The gentle Count a conqueror has died...."</span>
<span class="i0">Then asking pardon for his sins, or great</span>
<span class="i0">Or small, he offers up his glove to God.</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span></div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_177">CLXXVII.</a></h4><div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">The Count Rollánd feels now his end approach.</span>
<span class="i0">Against a pointed rock, and facing Spain,</span>
<span class="pagenum">[125]</span><span class="i0">He lies. Three times he beats his breast, and says:</span>
<span class="i0">"Mea culpa! Oh, my God, may through thy grace,</span>
<span class="i0">Be pardoned all my sins, or great or small,</span>
<span class="i0">Until this hour committed since my birth!"</span>
<span class="i0">Then his right glove he offers up to God,</span>
<span class="i0">And toward him angels from high Heav'n descend.</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span></div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_178">CLXXVIII.</a></h4><div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">Beneath a pine Rollánd doth lie, and looks</span>
<span class="i0">Toward Spain—He broods on many things of yore:</span>
<span class="i0">On all the lands he conquered, on sweet France,</span>
<span class="i0">On all his kinsmen, on great Carle his lord</span>
<span class="i0">Who nurtured him;—he sighs—nor can restrain</span>
<span class="i0">His tears, but can not yet himself forget;</span>
<span class="i0">Recalls his sins, and for the grace of God</span>
<span class="i0">He prays:—"Our Father, never yet untrue,</span>
<span class="i0">Who Saint-Lazare raised from the dead, and saved</span>
<span class="i0">Thy Daniel from the lions' claws—Oh, free</span>
<span class="i0">My soul from peril, from my whole life's sins!"</span>
<span class="i0">His right hand glove he offered up to God;</span>
<span class="i0">Saint Gabriel took the glove.—With head reclined</span>
<span class="pagenum">[126]</span><span class="i0">Upon his arm, with hands devoutly joined</span>
<span class="i0">He breathed his last. God sent his Cherubim,</span>
<span class="i0">Saint-Raphaël, <i>Saint Michiel del Peril</i>.</span>
<span class="i0">Together with them Gabriel came.—All bring</span>
<span class="i0">The soul of Count Rollánd to Paradise....</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span></div></div>
<hr style="width: 50%;">
<p><span class="pagenum">[127]</span></p>
<h4>THE CHASTISEMENT OF THE SARACENS.</h4>
<hr class="two">
<h4> <a id="St_179">CLXXIX.</a></h4><div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">Rollánd is dead: God has his soul in heaven.</span>
<span class="i0">To Ronceval the Emperor has come.</span>
<span class="i0">There, neither road nor any path is seen,</span>
<span class="i0">Nor vacant space, nor ell, nor foot of land</span>
<span class="i0">That mounds of mangled bodies cover not,</span>
<span class="i0">Pagans or French.—The Emperor exclaims:</span>
<span class="i0">"Fair nephew, where art thou? The Archbishop, where?</span>
<span class="i0">And Olivier, alas, where are they all?</span>
<span class="i0">Gerin, Gerier, the two companions, where</span>
<span class="i0">Are they? And where is Otes and Berengier,</span>
<span class="i0">Ives and Ivoire both to my heart so dear?</span>
<span class="i0">The Gascuin Engelier, Sansun the Duke,</span>
<span class="i0">Anseïs the rash, Gerard de Roussillon</span>
<span class="i0">The old, and my twelve Peers I left behind,</span>
<span class="pagenum">[128]</span><span class="i0">What fate is theirs?"—What boots it? None replies."—</span>
<span class="i0">"—God,"cries the King, "what grief is mine to think</span>
<span class="i0">"I stood not here the battle to begin."</span>
<span class="i0">He tears his beard with anger; all his knights</span>
<span class="i0">And barons weep great tears; dizzy with woe</span>
<span class="i0">And swooning, twenty thousand fall to earth.</span>
<span class="i0">Duke Naimes feels pity overflow his heart.</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span></div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_180">CLXXX.</a></h4><div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">No baron is there now, no chevalier</span>
<span class="i0">Who, in his pity, sheds not tears for sons,</span>
<span class="i0">For brothers—nephews—friends—and for liege-lords.</span>
<span class="i0">Many have fallen swooning on the earth,</span>
<span class="i0">But Duke Naimes bore himself as valorous knight:</span>
<span class="i0">He foremost said to Carle:—"Behold two leagues</span>
<span class="i0">Away!—The roads are dark with clouds of dust.</span>
<span class="i0">There swarm the Pagan tribes.... Ride on them now,</span>
<span class="i0">Avenge this bitter woe."—"O God," said Carle,</span>
<span class="i0">"Are they already flown so far?—our rights</span>
<span class="i0">And honor shield! Those Pagans took from me</span>
<span class="pagenum">[129]</span><span class="i0">The flower of my Sweet France!"—The King commands</span>
<span class="i0">Gebuin, Otun, Tedbalt de Reins and Count</span>
<span class="i0">Milun:—"Watch ye the field, the vales, the mounts;</span>
<span class="i0">The slain, leave to their rest; see that no beast</span>
<span class="i0">Nor lion, squire nor page approach. I charge</span>
<span class="i0">You, let no man upon them lay his hand</span>
<span class="i0">Until, with God's assistance, we return."</span>
<span class="i0">They lovingly and with sweet tone reply:</span>
<span class="i0">"Thus shall we do, just Emperor, dear sire!"</span>
<span class="i0">Upon the field they keep one thousand knights.</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span></div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_181">CLXXXI.</a></h4><div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">Now bids the Emperor his trumpets blow,</span>
<span class="i0">Then forward at the head of his great host</span>
<span class="i0">He rides, that Baron true. Of those of Spain</span>
<span class="i0">He finds the tracks, points out the road; in quick</span>
<span class="i0">Pursuit all follow Carle.... When sees the King</span>
<span class="i0">The eve decline, he on the verdant grass</span>
<span class="i0">Dismounts, and prostrate prays to God our Lord</span>
<span class="i0">The sun to stay, the shades of night hold back</span>
<span class="i0">And longer make the day. To him appears</span>
<span class="i0">A Counselor-Angel with the swift command;</span>
<span class="i0">"Ride on, O King, nor fear that night shall fall!</span>
<span class="pagenum">[130]</span><span class="i0">God knows that thou hast lost the flower of France;</span>
<span class="i0">But vengeance canst have now upon that horde</span>
<span class="i0">Of unbelievers." Thus the Angel spake.</span>
<span class="i0">The Emp'ror rises and remounts his steed.</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span></div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_182">CLXXXII.</a></h4><div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">To Carlemagne Our Lord now showed his might;</span>
<span class="i0">The sun stays in its course. The Pagans fly,</span>
<span class="i0">And fast the French pursuing, overtake</span>
<span class="i0">Them in the Val-Tenebre. They drive them on</span>
<span class="i0">Toward Sarraguce, while close behind them fall</span>
<span class="i0">The upraised swords, and strew the ground with dead.</span>
<span class="i0">No issue, no escape, by road or pass!</span>
<span class="i0">In front deep Ebro rolls its mighty waves:</span>
<span class="i0">No boat, no barge, no raft. They call for help</span>
<span class="i0">On Tervagant, then plunge into the flood.</span>
<span class="i0">Vain was their trust: some, weighted with their arms,</span>
<span class="i0">Sink in a moment; others are swept down,</span>
<span class="i0">And those most favored swallow monstrous draughts.</span>
<span class="i0">All drown most cruelly. The French cry out:</span>
<span class="i0">"For your own woe wished ye to see Rollánd!"</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span></div></div>
<p><span class="pagenum">[131]</span></p>
<h4> <a id="St_183">CLXXXIII.</a></h4><div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">When Carle sees all the Pagans dead—some slain,</span>
<span class="i0">The others drowned, his chevaliers enriched</span>
<span class="i0">With spoils, the noble King dismounts, on earth</span>
<span class="i0">Prostrates himself and offers thanks to God.</span>
<span class="i0">When he arose, the sun had set. "'Tis time,"</span>
<span class="i0">He said, "to think of camping now. Too late</span>
<span class="i0">It is for our advance to Ronceval.</span>
<span class="i0">Our horses are all weary and foredone:</span>
<span class="i0">Unsaddle them and take the bridles off;</span>
<span class="i0">And let them roam at large about these meads."</span>
<span class="i0">The French reply: "Sire, you have spoken well."</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span></div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_184">CLXXXIV.</a></h4><div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">The Emperor makes here his harborage.</span>
<span class="i0">The French dismount, take off the golden curbs</span>
<span class="i0">And saddles from their steeds, and turn them loose</span>
<span class="i0">In the green mead, amid the plenteous grass:</span>
<span class="i0">No other care they need. Upon the ground</span>
<span class="i0">The over-wearied cast themselves and sleep.</span>
<span class="i0">No watch was set in all the host that night.</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span></div></div>
<p><span class="pagenum">[132]</span></p>
<h4> <a id="St_185">CLXXXV.</a></h4><div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">The Emperor reposes on the field,</span>
<span class="i0">His mighty lance hard by his pillow planted,</span>
<span class="i0">For he, on such a night will not disarm.</span>
<span class="i0">His hauberk white, with orfreyed-marge he wears,</span>
<span class="i0">His helmet, rich with gold and gems is laced,</span>
<span class="i0">Girded Joyeuse, the sword without a peer,</span>
<span class="i0">Who thirty times a day can change his hue.</span>
<span class="i0">Many a time you all heard of the lance</span>
<span class="i0">Wherewith Our Lord was pierced upon the cross,</span>
<span class="i0">The steel whereof Carle has, thanks be to God,</span>
<span class="i0">Closed in the golden pommel of his sword.</span>
<span class="i0">For this great glory and exceeding worth</span>
<span class="i0">The brand was called <i>Joyeuse</i>. This all French Knights</span>
<span class="i0">Should bear in mind, for it was hence they took</span>
<span class="i0">Their war-cry of <i>Montjoie</i>, and for this cause</span>
<span class="i0">No other people can resist their arms.</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span></div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_186">CLXXXVI.</a></h4><div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">Clear is the night, bright shines the moon; at rest</span>
<span class="i0">Lies Carle; but grief is with him for Rollánd,</span>
<span class="i0">And Olivier is heavy on his heart;</span>
<span class="pagenum">[133]</span><span class="i0">The twelve Peers, too, and all the men of France,</span>
<span class="i0">Left stark and bloody there at Ronceval.</span>
<span class="i0">He cannot help but weep, and sob, and pray</span>
<span class="i0">That mighty God be keeper of their souls.</span>
<span class="i0">Tired is the King, his toils being very great;</span>
<span class="i0">Deeply asleep he falls, and can no more.</span>
<span class="i0">Through all the fields the scattered French sleep sound,</span>
<span class="i0">Nor there a horse has strength enough to stand;</span>
<span class="i0">If one need grass, he bites it as he lies.</span>
<span class="i0">Right wise is he that's wise in lore of woe.</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span></div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_187">CLXXXVII.</a></h4><div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">Carle sleeps as man by toil outdone. God sends</span>
<span class="i0">Saint Gabriel down, the Emperor to guard.</span>
<span class="i0">All night beside his head the Angel stands,</span>
<span class="i0">And in a dream forebodes that 'gainst the French</span>
<span class="i0">A battle is prepared, and its portent</span>
<span class="i0">Explains; then glancing up tow'rd Heav'n, King Carle</span>
<span class="i0">Sees thunder-clouds and winds, hail, raging storms</span>
<span class="i0">And wond'rous tempests—smould'ring fire and flames</span>
<span class="i0">Ready to burst forth. Suddenly on all</span>
<span class="pagenum">[134]</span><span class="i0">His people falls the blast. Their spears with shafts</span>
<span class="i0">Of apple-tree or ash—those shields ablaze</span>
<span class="i0">Unto their golden rings—shafts from their points</span>
<span class="i0">Break off—Steel helms and hauberks clash and clang.</span>
<span class="i0">He sees his Knights in dire distress. Meantime</span>
<span class="i0">Devouring pards and bears rush on them; snakes</span>
<span class="i0">And vipers—dragons, fiends—and with them more</span>
<span class="i0">Than thirty thousand griffons. 'Mong the French</span>
<span class="i0">None can escape this hideous horde.—"Carlemagne,</span>
<span class="i0">Come to our help!" they cry. With pity seized,</span>
<span class="i0">Fain would he thither, but his steps are stayed:</span>
<span class="i0">Deep from a wood a lion huge comes on.</span>
<span class="i0">The beast is haughty, fierce and terrible,</span>
<span class="i0">And, springing, seeks his very body out.</span>
<span class="i0">Each wrestles with the other in his arms;</span>
<span class="i0">But which shall fall, which stand, this no man knows.</span>
<span class="i0">Never a jot the Emperor awakes.</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span></div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_188">CLXXXVIII.</a></h4><div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">Another vision follows this: in France</span>
<span class="i0">At Aix he is:—Upon a marble step</span>
<span class="pagenum">[135]</span><span class="i0">He stands, and holds in two-fold chains a bear.</span>
<span class="i0">From towards Ardennes he sees rush forth a pack</span>
<span class="i0">Of thirty other bears which speak as men.</span>
<span class="i0">They say:—"To us restore him, Sire! Not right</span>
<span class="i0">It were that you should keep him longer; help</span>
<span class="i0">Our kin we must."—Then from his palace runs</span>
<span class="i0">A greyhound fair which on the verdant grass</span>
<span class="i0">Assails the fiercer of the other beasts</span>
<span class="i0">Before them all. The King a wond'rous fight</span>
<span class="i0">Beholds: but who shall win or lose, none knows.</span>
<span class="i0">This is a dream God's Angel showed to Carle,</span>
<span class="i0">Who sleeps until the morrow's morn appeared.</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span></div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_189">CLXXXIX.</a></h4><div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">By rapid flight Marsile reached Sarraguce.—</span>
<span class="i0">Dismounting 'neath a shady olive-tree,</span>
<span class="i0">He strips himself of breast-plate, helmet, sword,</span>
<span class="i0">And sinks upon the sward with ghastly look.</span>
<span class="i0">His right hand severed from the wrist whence blood</span>
<span class="i0">Is gushing forth, has made him swoon with pain.</span>
<span class="i0">Before Marsile, his spouse, Queen Bramimunde,</span>
<span class="i0">Bursts into tears, and cries, and woeful moans.</span>
<span class="i0">Around stand more than twenty thousand men</span>
<span class="pagenum">[136]</span><span class="i0">Who with one voice accuse Sweet France and Carle;</span>
<span class="i0">Apollo's grotto seek they, and with taunts,</span>
<span class="i0">Profane, insulting words, their God revile:</span>
<span class="i0">"What ails thee, evil God, to shame us thus,</span>
<span class="i0">And to confusion bring our Lord the King?</span>
<span class="i0">Who serves thee well vile guerdon gains from thee!"</span>
<span class="i0">Despoiled of crown and scepter, by the hands</span>
<span class="i0">They hang him on a column—neath their feet</span>
<span class="i0">They roll him down.—They with great clubs deface</span>
<span class="i0">And beat him; then from Tervagant they snatch</span>
<span class="i0">His carbuncle; Mohamed in a ditch</span>
<span class="i0">Throw down—there bitt'n, trampled on, by swine and dogs.</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span></div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_190">CXC.</a></h4><div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">Recov'ring from his swoon, the King Marsile</span>
<span class="i0">Commands they lead him to his vaulted room</span>
<span class="i0">All bright with color and inscribed with verse.</span>
<span class="i0">There weeping bitterly, Queen Bramimunde</span>
<span class="i0">Tearing her hair, aloud proclaims her grief:</span>
<span class="i0">"O hapless Sarraguce, thou art bereft</span>
<span class="i0">Of the most gentle King that was thy Lord!</span>
<span class="pagenum">[137]</span><span class="i0">Our gods betrayed our trust, they who this morn</span>
<span class="i0">In battle failed us;—the Emir coward were</span>
<span class="i0">Would he not fight these people bold who are</span>
<span class="i0">So proud they care not for their lives. Carl'magne,</span>
<span class="i0">The Emperor, whose beard is strewn with gray,</span>
<span class="i0">Among his men has dauntless Knights; if e'er</span>
<span class="i0">He fight, no step he yields. Great woe it is</span>
<span class="i0">That there is no man who can give him death."</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span></div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_191">CXCI.</a></h4><div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">By his great power the Emperor in Spain</span>
<span class="i0">Full seven years remained; he castles took</span>
<span class="i0">And many cities, bringing sore distress</span>
<span class="i0">To King Marsile. The year had scarce begun</span>
<span class="i0">Before his word went forth to seal the briefs</span>
<span class="i0">Which summoned Baligant from Babylone,</span>
<span class="i0">(The aged Emir, he whose life outlived</span>
<span class="i0">Homer and Virgil). Now the King Marsile</span>
<span class="i0">Had begged the Baron's help for Sarraguce.</span>
<span class="i0">Should he not come, gods, idols, once adored</span>
<span class="i0">He will renounce, the holy Faith of Christ</span>
<span class="i0">Embrace, and join in friendship with King Carle.</span>
<span class="i0">Afar was Baligant, and tarried long;</span>
<span class="i0">From forty realms his people had he called</span>
<span class="pagenum">[138]</span><span class="i0">And ordered to prepare his <i>dromonds</i> vast,</span>
<span class="i0">Barks, galleys, ev'ry vessel. In the port</span>
<span class="i0">Of Alexandria the fleet had met;</span>
<span class="i0">In May it was, the first of summer-days,</span>
<span class="i0">A mighty host he launched upon the deep.</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span></div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_192">CXCII.</a></h4><div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">Great are the forces of their hostile horde;</span>
<span class="i0">They swiftly skim the waves, and steer, and sail;</span>
<span class="i0">Their masts and yards so blazing with the light</span>
<span class="i0">Of carbuncles and lanterns, night gives up</span>
<span class="i0">Its darkness and still fairer shows the sea.</span>
<span class="i0">As they approached the shores of Spain, the land</span>
<span class="i0">Was all aglow, and tidings reached Marsile.</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span></div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_193">CXCIII.</a></h4><div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">The Pagans halt no moment; soon they leave</span>
<span class="i0">The deep, and in fresh water steer; Marbreise</span>
<span class="i0">And then Marbruse is passed; along the shores</span>
<span class="i0">Of winding Ebro glides the armament,</span>
<span class="i0">Setting the night aflame with carbuncles</span>
<span class="i0">And lights: the same day reached they Sarraguce.</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span></div></div>
<p><span class="pagenum">[139]</span></p>
<h4> <a id="St_194">CXCIV.</a></h4><div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">Clear is the day and bright the sun; descends</span>
<span class="i0">The Emir from his ship. Espaneliz</span>
<span class="i0">Walks forth upon his right; a train of Kings</span>
<span class="i0">In number seventeen, with Dukes and Counts</span>
<span class="i0">Innumerable, follow. 'Mid the plain</span>
<span class="i0">Grows a great laurel, and beneath its shade</span>
<span class="i0">They spread a <i>pallie</i> of white silk upon</span>
<span class="i0">The verdant grass, and place a faldstool there</span>
<span class="i0">Of ivory. In this sits Baligant</span>
<span class="i0">The Pagan. All the others stand. First spake</span>
<span class="i0">The chief:—"Oyez, all ye, most valiant Knights!</span>
<span class="i0">King Carle, the Emperor, who leads the Franks,</span>
<span class="i0">Shall eat not, save by my command. Throughout</span>
<span class="i0">All Spain, 'gainst me a cruel war he waged:</span>
<span class="i0">Now I will seek him in sweet France, nor, while</span>
<span class="i0">My life lasts, cease until he dies the death,</span>
<span class="i0">Or, living, yields, and mercy begs." He spake</span>
<span class="i0">And struck his right-hand glove upon his knee.</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span></div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_195">CXCV.</a></h4><div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">His word once spoken was to him a law:</span>
<span class="i0">Though it cost all the gold beneath the sky,</span>
<span class="pagenum">[140]</span><span class="i0">Yet would he march to Aix, where Carle was wont</span>
<span class="i0">To hold his court. Some praise him, even give</span>
<span class="i0">Him counsel. Two from out his host of Knights</span>
<span class="i0">He summons, Clarien, and Clarifan:</span>
<span class="i0">"Ye are the sons of King Maltraïen,</span>
<span class="i0">A willing message bearer: 'tis my will</span>
<span class="i0">Ye go to Sarraguce; there in my name</span>
<span class="i0">Give ye this message to the King Marsile:</span>
<span class="i0">I have come to succor him against the French,</span>
<span class="i0">And if I find them, great the fight will be.</span>
<span class="i0">Give him this gold-embroidered glove, and place it</span>
<span class="i0">On his right hand; give him this staff of gold;</span>
<span class="i0">And when he comes to pay me homage, as</span>
<span class="i0">A vassal to his lord, I then will lead</span>
<span class="i0">My force to France to fight with Carlemagne.</span>
<span class="i0">If he fall not before my feet to pray</span>
<span class="i0">For mercy, and abjure the Christian law,</span>
<span class="i0">I from his head will tear away the crown."</span>
<span class="i0">The Pagans answer all:—"Well spoken, Sire."</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span></div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_196">CXCVI.</a></h4><div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">"Barons! to horse!" said Baligant. "Bear thou</span>
<span class="i0">The glove, and thou the staff." The two reply:</span>
<span class="i0">"Dear Sire, thus shall we do." So fast they rode</span>
<span class="pagenum">[141]</span><span class="i0">They soon reached Sarraguce. Beneath ten gates</span>
<span class="i0">They pass, four bridges cross, ride through the streets</span>
<span class="i0">Where stand the burghers. But on drawing near</span>
<span class="i0">The lofty citadel, they heard great noise</span>
<span class="i0">About the palace, where were thronging crowds</span>
<span class="i0">Of Pagans with loud wails and shrieks of woe,</span>
<span class="i0">Crying out against their gods, on Tervagan,</span>
<span class="i0">Mahum, Apollo, who avail them naught.</span>
<span class="i0">Each says to each, "Ah, caitiffs, what shall now</span>
<span class="i0">Befall us, miserable? for we have lost</span>
<span class="i0">The King Marsile whose hand Rollánd struck off;</span>
<span class="i0">For aye we are bereft of Turfaleu</span>
<span class="i0">The Fair, his son. This day the land of Spain</span>
<span class="i0">Into the Christian hands will fall enslaved!"</span>
<span class="i0">The message-bearers reach the royal gates.</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span></div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_197">CXCVII.</a></h4><div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">Beneath an olive tree they halt, and soon</span>
<span class="i0">Two Pagans take their curbed steeds in charge.</span>
<span class="i0">The messengers, each holding by the cloak</span>
<span class="i0">The other, hasten to the highest tower.</span>
<span class="i0">Entering the vaulted hall where lay Marsile,</span>
<span class="i0">An evil greeting offer with good will:</span>
<span class="pagenum">[142]</span><span class="i0">"May Tervagan, Apollo, he who holds</span>
<span class="i0">Us in his service, and our Sire Mahum,</span>
<span class="i0">Preserve our king and guard the queen!"</span>
<span class="i0">Whereat cried Bramimunde:—"What folly this!</span>
<span class="i0">Our gods are false; too well in Ronceval</span>
<span class="i0">They showed their evil power, and let our knights</span>
<span class="i0">Be slain—amid the battle-field forsook</span>
<span class="i0">My lord the king with his right hand struck off</span>
<span class="i0">By mighty Count Rollánd. The realm of Spain</span>
<span class="i0">Will fall enslaved beneath the sway of Carle.</span>
<span class="i0">What shall become of me, most miserable?</span>
<span class="i0">Alas! is there no man to give me death!"</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span></div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_198">CXCVIII.</a></h4><div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">Said Clarien:—"Lady, speak not thus—Behold,</span>
<span class="i0">Messengers we, from Baligant, who swears</span>
<span class="i0">To free Marsile, and to him sends his glove</span>
<span class="i0">And staff as tokens—on the Ebro float</span>
<span class="i0">Four thousand galleys, skiffs and swiftest boats;</span>
<span class="i0">More sails than can be numbered! Rich and great</span>
<span class="i0">The Emir.—Carle, pursued to France, shall be</span>
<span class="i0">Per force, or still, or dead, or penitent."</span>
<span class="i0">Said Bramimunde:—"Yea, greater ills will come.</span>
<span class="i0">To meet the Franks you need not go so far;</span>
<span class="pagenum">[143]</span>
<span class="i0">Carle seven years in Spain has tarried. Brave</span>
<span class="i0">Is he in battle, and a Baron true;</span>
<span class="i0">Ready to die ere he will quit the field;</span>
<span class="i0">No king on earth but is to him a child.</span>
<span class="i0">Carle's spirit yields before no living man."</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span></div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_199">CXCIX.</a></h4><div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">"Let all that be!" cried to the messengers</span>
<span class="i0">The King Marsile—"Seigneurs, speak but to me,</span>
<span class="i0">You see me now crushed unto death. No son</span>
<span class="i0">Nor daughter have I left, nor other heir;</span>
<span class="i0">One son I had, who yestereve was slain.</span>
<span class="i0">Say to my Lord his coming I beseech.</span>
<span class="i0">Some rights to Spain the Emir has; to him</span>
<span class="i0">I grant the realm in full, if he accept.</span>
<span class="i0">Let him defend this land against the French,</span>
<span class="i0">To meet Carlemagne good counsel I will give,</span>
<span class="i0">And victor he will be before this day</span>
<span class="i0">A month. Bear him the keys of Sarraguce;</span>
<span class="i0">Thence, if he trust my words, he ne'er will be</span>
<span class="i0">Expelled." They answer:—"Sire, you speak the truth."</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span></div></div>
<p><span class="pagenum">[144]</span></p>
<h4> <a id="St_200">CC.</a></h4><div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">"The Emperor Carle," said King Marsile, "has slain</span>
<span class="i0">My men, ravaged my land, shattered and stormed</span>
<span class="i0">My cities; now on Ebro's banks he camps,</span>
<span class="i0">But seven counted leagues away. Bid ye</span>
<span class="i0">The Emir march up all his force. Bear him</span>
<span class="i0">My order for the fight." With this he gives</span>
<span class="i0">Into their hands the keys of Sarraguce.</span>
<span class="i0">Upon these words the messengers bent low</span>
<span class="i0">In last salute, took leave, and went their way.</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span></div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_201">CCI.</a></h4><div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">The messengers upon their horses mount</span>
<span class="i0">And gallop from the city in hot haste.</span>
<span class="i0">With terror struck, both to the Emir come,</span>
<span class="i0">Deliv'ring up the keys of Sarraguce.</span>
<span class="i0">Said Baligant:—"What found ye there? Where is</span>
<span class="i0">The King Marsile whom I commanded forth?"</span>
<span class="i0">Clarien makes answer:—"He is hurt to death;</span>
<span class="i0">The Emp'ror yesterday marched through the pass</span>
<span class="i0">Upon his homeward way into sweet France.</span>
<span class="i0">For greater honor, in the rear, Rollánd,</span>
<span class="i0">His nephew, had a post with Olivier,</span>
<span class="i0">All the twelve Peers and twenty thousand knights.</span>
<span class="i0">The King Marsile, the valiant Baron, fought</span>
<span class="i0">And fierce encounter had with Count Rollánd,</span>
<span class="pagenum">[145]</span><span class="i0">Who dealt with Durendal so dire a blow,</span>
<span class="i0">The king's right hand was severed from his arm.</span>
<span class="i0">Slain was the son he loved so tenderly,</span>
<span class="i0">With all the Barons he had brought with him;</span>
<span class="i0">Unable to resist, he took to flight,</span>
<span class="i0">And Carle, the Emperor, followed close behind.</span>
<span class="i0">Now give your help to King Marsile, who craves</span>
<span class="i0">Your aid, and as your guerdon all the realm</span>
<span class="i0">Of Spain receive." But Baligant remains</span>
<span class="i0">Deep sunk in thought, nigh maddened by his grief.</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span></div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_202">CCII.</a></h4><div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">"Sire Emir," Clarien said, "on yesterday</span>
<span class="i0">A battle raged in Ronceval; Rollánd</span>
<span class="i0">And Olivier are dead, and the twelve Peers</span>
<span class="i0">To Carle so dear, with twenty thousand Franks</span>
<span class="i0">Have perished; King Marsile lost his right hand,</span>
<span class="i0">And fled in hottest speed pursued by Carle.</span>
<span class="i0">In all the land no Knight remains but slain</span>
<span class="i0">Or in the waters of the Ebro drowned.</span>
<span class="i0">Upon its banks the French encamp—So nigh—</span>
<span class="i0">Had you the will, unsafe would be their flight."</span>
<span class="i0">Then Baligant looks at him full of pride;</span>
<span class="i0">And his heart swells with courage and fierce joy.</span>
<span class="pagenum">[146]</span><span class="i0">Sudden from his footstool he springs, and loud</span>
<span class="i0">He cries:—"Delay not—disembark! To horse!</span>
<span class="i0">And forward! Now, unless Carlemagne the old</span>
<span class="i0">By flight escape, the King Marsile shall be</span>
<span class="i0">Avenged. For his right hand Carle's head shall pay."</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span></div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_203">CCIII.</a></h4><div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">Out of their skiffs the Arab Pagans spring,</span>
<span class="i0">And mounting mules and horses, march; what else</span>
<span class="i0">But this for them to do? When forward moves</span>
<span class="i0">The host in serried lines, the Emir calls</span>
<span class="i0">On Genalfin, his chosen friend: "To thee</span>
<span class="i0">Command of all my armies I confide."—</span>
<span class="i0">He said—and straight on his bay destrier mounts;</span>
<span class="i0">Four Dukes rode with him, and so fast he sped,</span>
<span class="i0">Ere long they entered into Sarraguce.</span>
<span class="i0">Before a marble terrace he dismounts,</span>
<span class="i0">Four Counts his stirrup held, and by the steps</span>
<span class="i0">Which led up to the palace he ascends.</span>
<span class="i0">To him runs Bramimunde:—"What cruel dole</span>
<span class="i0">Is mine, oh, woe! How shamefully," she cried,</span>
<span class="i0">"Have I now lost my lord!"—And at his feet</span>
<span class="i0">Prostrate she fell. The Emir raised her up,</span>
<span class="i0">And, grieving, both into the chamber went.</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span></div></div>
<p><span class="pagenum">[147]</span></p>
<h4> <a id="St_204">CCIV.</a></h4><div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">The King Marsile, on seeing Baligant,</span>
<span class="i0">Summoned two Spanish Saracens, and bade</span>
<span class="i0">His body to be raised that he might sit.</span>
<span class="i0">With his left hand he took a glove, and thus</span>
<span class="i0">He spoke:—"Sir King and Emir, all my lands</span>
<span class="i0">And kingdoms, Sarraguce, domains and fiefs</span>
<span class="i0">But wreck and ruin—Subjects, wealth—all lost."</span>
<span class="i0">Answered the Emir:—"I, so much the more,</span>
<span class="i0">Grieve for thy sorrow; but for longer speech</span>
<span class="i0">I can not stay; for Carle, I know, will not</span>
<span class="i0">Be still. But, nathless, I receive the glove."</span>
<span class="i0">O'erwhelmed with sorrow, weeping he departs;</span>
<span class="i0">The palace steps descending, mounts his horse</span>
<span class="i0">And spurs him towards the waiting hosts so fast,</span>
<span class="i0">That of the foremost ranks he takes the lead;</span>
<span class="i0">And cries aloud, going from man to man:</span>
<span class="i0">"Haste, Pagans! On!—Already flee the Franks."</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span></div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_205">CCV.</a></h4><div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">At earliest morn, just as the dawn appeared,</span>
<span class="i0">From sleep awakes the Emp'ror Carlemagne;</span>
<span class="i0">Saint-Gabriel, his guardian, sent by God,</span>
<span class="pagenum">[148]</span><span class="i0">With hands uplifted signed him with the cross.</span>
<span class="i0">The King arises, takes his armor off,</span>
<span class="i0">And all the host disarm.—The mounted knights</span>
<span class="i0">Then ran at speed back o'er the trampled ways,</span>
<span class="i0">The weary roads, to view the woeful loss</span>
<span class="i0">Once more, on Ronceval's bloody battle-field.</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span></div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_206">CCVI.</a></h4><div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">Arrived upon the field of Ronceval,</span>
<span class="i0">Where lay so many slain, Carle wept, and said</span>
<span class="i0">Unto the French:—"Seigneurs, move slowly here;</span>
<span class="i0">For I alone, will forward go in search</span>
<span class="i0">Of my fair nephew lost among the dead.</span>
<span class="i0">Erst when at Aix on Christmas' solemn feast,</span>
<span class="i0">My valiant bachelors, in warlike deeds</span>
<span class="i0">Their exploits vaunting, I could hear Rollánd</span>
<span class="i0">Say, should he ever die on foreign soil,</span>
<span class="i0">Before his peers and men he should be found</span>
<span class="i0">Facing the foe, true Baron, conqu'ror still."</span>
<span class="i0">A few steps further than a staff's throw, Carle</span>
<span class="i0">Far in advance of all, ascends a hill.</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span></div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_207">CCVII.</a></h4><div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">When sought the Emperor his nephew there,</span>
<span class="i0">Amid the field, and found so many plants</span>
<span class="i0">With blossoms crimsoned by our Barons' blood,</span>
<span class="pagenum">[149]</span><span class="i0">By pity moved he can not choose but weep.</span>
<span class="i0">Mounting the hill, beneath two trees, he knew</span>
<span class="i0">The blow upon the three rocks Rollánd struck,</span>
<span class="i0">And saw his nephew lying on the sward,</span>
<span class="i0">A mangled corse—No wonder Carle is wroth;</span>
<span class="i0">Alights in haste and lifting in his arms</span>
<span class="i0">The Count, broken by grief upon him faints.</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span></div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_208">CCVIII.</a></h4><div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">From his deep swoon the Emperor revives.</span>
<span class="i0">Duke Naimes, Count Acelin, Geffrei d'Anjou</span>
<span class="i0">His brother Tierri raise the King, and place</span>
<span class="i0">Him resting 'gainst a pine. There on the earth</span>
<span class="i0">He sees his nephew lying dead, and mourns</span>
<span class="i0">O'er him with gentle words and tender looks,</span>
<span class="i0">"Sweet friend, Rollánd, God's mercy unto thee!</span>
<span class="i0">Such peerless knight none ever yet has seen,</span>
<span class="i0">For noble combats ordered and achieved!</span>
<span class="i0">Mine honor turns to its decline!—" Once more</span>
<span class="i0">Carle's will and strength succumb.... He faints away.</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span></div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_209">CCIX.</a></h4><div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">Again King Carle recovers from his swoon....</span>
<span class="i0">Four of his Barons, with their hands support</span>
<span class="pagenum">[150]</span><span class="i0">His form. His downcast looks see stretched on earth</span>
<span class="i0">His nephew's corpse. Discolored was the brow,</span>
<span class="i0">Yet proud the look; the dimmed and sightless eyes</span>
<span class="i0">Turned up.... In faith and love King Carle laments.</span>
<span class="i0">"Sweet friend Rollánd, may God enshrine thy soul</span>
<span class="i0">Among the Glorified, amidst the flowers</span>
<span class="i0">Of Paradise! For thy mishap, Seigneur,</span>
<span class="i0">Camest thou to Spain.... No future day shall dawn</span>
<span class="i0">For me, on which I mourn thee not.... Now fall'n</span>
<span class="i0">My strength and power! Who now will e'er support</span>
<span class="i0">My royal fiefs? Thou wast for me 'neath Heav'n</span>
<span class="i0">The one true friend! though other kindred mine,</span>
<span class="i0">Was none so brave and wise."—He tore his hair</span>
<span class="i0">In handfuls from his brow. So great the grief</span>
<span class="i0">Of those one hundred thousand Franks, that none</span>
<span class="i0">There was, of all, who wept not bitter tears.</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span></div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_210">CCX.</a></h4><div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">"Beloved Rollánd, to France I now return.</span>
<span class="i0">When in my chamber I shall be at Loün,</span>
<span class="pagenum">[151]</span><span class="i0">And foreign men come from afar to ask</span>
<span class="i0">Where lives Rollánd the Captain, I shall say</span>
<span class="i0">'He lieth dead in Spain;' and I henceforth</span>
<span class="i0">Shall hold my realm in bitter pain. No day</span>
<span class="i0">Shall dawn for me unmarked by tears and moans."</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span></div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_211">CCXI.</a></h4><div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">"Sweet friend Rollánd, brave Knight and beauteous youth,</span>
<span class="i0">When I return to Aix, in my Chapelle,</span>
<span class="i0">And men shall come to hear me speak of thee,</span>
<span class="i0">What strange and cruel news I then shall have</span>
<span class="i0">To greet them with! 'My nephew who for me</span>
<span class="i0">Such conquests made ... is dead.' And Saxons now</span>
<span class="i0">Will rise against my power, and Hungres, and Bugres</span>
<span class="i0">With other foes—the men of Rome, of Pouille,</span>
<span class="i0">And all those of Palerne; and those who hold</span>
<span class="i0">Affrike and Califerne. Day after day</span>
<span class="i0">My pain will grow—Who then shall lead my host</span>
<span class="i0">With such an arm of might, since he is dead,</span>
<span class="i0">Who was our chief and head so long. Alas!</span>
<span class="pagenum">[152]</span><span class="i0">Sweet France, bereft art thou! So great my grief</span>
<span class="i0">I would not live!"—he plucks out his white beard</span>
<span class="i0">And tears his hair with both hands from his head.</span>
<span class="i0">Swoon on the earth one hundred thousand Franks—</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span></div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_212">CCXII.</a></h4><div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">"Sweet friend," he cried, "Rollánd, thou art no more:</span>
<span class="i0">Oh! may thy soul have place in Paradise!</span>
<span class="i0">Who gave thee death brought grievous shame to France.</span>
<span class="i0">Such is my grief, I would not longer live.</span>
<span class="i0">My kinsmen died for me! I pray Our Lord,</span>
<span class="i0">The Blessed Mary's son, before I reach</span>
<span class="i0">Cizra's defiles, from mortal life to take</span>
<span class="i0">My soul away, and let it rest with theirs.</span>
<span class="i0">I would my body lay beside their own!"</span>
<span class="i0">And, weeping sore, he tears his hoary beard....</span>
<span class="i0">Then said Duke Naimes:—"What cruel pain is Carle's!"</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span></div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_213">CCXIII.</a></h4><div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">"Sire Emperor," spoke forth Geffrei d'Anjou,</span>
<span class="i0">"Yield not so much to sorrow—Orders give</span>
<span class="pagenum">[153]</span><span class="i0">To seek our men throughout the battle-field,</span>
<span class="i0">In combat killed by those of Spain, and lay</span>
<span class="i0">Them in one grave"—Carle said: "Then sound your horn."</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span></div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_214">CCXIV.</a></h4><div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">Geffrei d'Anjou obeyed and blew his horn;</span>
<span class="i0">The French dismount, such was the king's command,</span>
<span class="i0">And all their friends found slain upon the field</span>
<span class="i0">Together in one charnel wide inter:</span>
<span class="i0">A crowd of bishops, abbots, canons, monks</span>
<span class="i0">And tonsured priests there gathered, in the name</span>
<span class="i0">Of God assoil and bless; incense and myrrh</span>
<span class="i0">Are burned in reverence and love before</span>
<span class="i0">The dead who, buried there with honors great,</span>
<span class="i0">Are left alone—what more was there to do?</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span></div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_215">CCXV.</a></h4><div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">The Emp'ror Carle gives order that a watch</span>
<span class="i0">Be kept around Rollánd, Count Olivier</span>
<span class="i0">And the Archbishop Turpin; bade their breasts</span>
<span class="i0">Be oped before him, and their hearts enwrapped</span>
<span class="pagenum">[154]</span>
<span class="i0">In silken cloths—in tombs of marble white</span>
<span class="i0">Inurned; the bodies of the Barons then</span>
<span class="i0">Perfumed with wine and fragrant herbs; the three</span>
<span class="i0">Seigneurs in wrappings of stag's hide were cased;</span>
<span class="i0">By Carle's decree Tedbald and Gebuin,</span>
<span class="i0"><i>Marchis</i> Othon and Count Milon escort</span>
<span class="i0">Them on their way, upon three chariots borne,</span>
<span class="i0">And covered well with palls of glazèd silk.</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span></div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_216">CCXVI.</a></h4><div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">King Carle about to start, sees suddenly</span>
<span class="i0">Emerge the Pagan van. From Baligant,</span>
<span class="i0">The battle to declare, two messengers</span>
<span class="i0">Advance:—"Proud king, from here thou must not go;</span>
<span class="i0">Behold, the Emir to thine encounter comes</span>
<span class="i0">And brings a mighty host from Araby.</span>
<span class="i0">This day will prove if truly valiant knight</span>
<span class="i0">Thou art." Carl'magne, the king, plucks his gray beard;</span>
<span class="i0">So cruel is the memory of all</span>
<span class="i0">His grief and wrong, proudly he casts a look</span>
<span class="i0">Upon his knightly host, and with loud voice</span>
<span class="i0">Exclaims:—"Seigneurs Barons! To horse! To arms!"</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span></div></div>
<p><span class="pagenum">[155]</span></p>
<h4> <a id="St_217">CCXVII.</a></h4><div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">First of them all the Emperor is armed.</span>
<span class="i0">Quick donned his hauberk,—laced his helm—Joyeuse,</span>
<span class="i0">Whose brightness vies with the sun's dazzling rays,</span>
<span class="i0">Is girded on—a shield of Girunde hangs</span>
<span class="i0">Upon his neck,—his lance, forged in Blandune</span>
<span class="i0">He wields, and mounts his good steed Tencendur</span>
<span class="i0">Which nigh the ford below Marsune he won,</span>
<span class="i0">When he struck dead Malpalin de Nerbune.</span>
<span class="i0">Quick to a gallop spurred, rein loosed, the steed</span>
<span class="i0">Sped on, before one hundred thousand men.</span>
<span class="i0">Carle calls on Rome's Apostle and on God.</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span></div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_218">CCXVIII.</a></h4><div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">Spread o'er the field the men of France dismount.</span>
<span class="i0">More than one hundred thousand arm themselves</span>
<span class="i0">Together—Brilliant their array! Their steeds</span>
<span class="i0">Are fleet, arms gleaming; bright the pennons float</span>
<span class="i0">Above their helms: The foe once found, they give</span>
<span class="i0">Them certain battle. Mounted thus, how brave</span>
<span class="pagenum">[156]</span><span class="i0">Their show! When Carle beholds their faces bright,</span>
<span class="i0">Joseran de Provence he calls, the brave</span>
<span class="i0">Duke Naimes, also Anselme de Maience:</span>
<span class="i0">"In knights so good behooves men to have faith,</span>
<span class="i0">And mad indeed who doubts of the event.</span>
<span class="i0">Should not the Arabs their approach repent,</span>
<span class="i0">Rollánd's death I to them will dearly sell."</span>
<span class="i0">Responds Duke Naimes:—"May God vouchsafe your prayer."</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span></div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_219">CCXIX.</a></h4><div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">Carl calls Rabel and Guineman:—"Seigneurs,</span>
<span class="i0">I will that ye should take the place of Counts</span>
<span class="i0">Rollánd and Olivier—One bear the sword;</span>
<span class="i0">The olifant, the other—Be the chiefs</span>
<span class="i0">Of fifteen thousand bachelors of France,</span>
<span class="i0">In youth and valor famous among all—</span>
<span class="i0">As many more will follow after these,</span>
<span class="i0">Conducted by Gebuin and by Laurant."</span>
<span class="i0">Duke Naimes and Joseran the Count with speed</span>
<span class="i0">And care these hosts in full array dispose.</span>
<span class="i0">Let them encounter, great will be the fight.</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span></div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_220">CCXX.</a></h4><div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">These first two cohorts were from out the French</span>
<span class="i0">Composed; and after those a third was formed:</span>
<span class="pagenum">[157]</span><span class="i0">The vassals of Baviere—Their numbers mount</span>
<span class="i0">To thirty thousand knights who ne'er would blench</span>
<span class="i0">Before the foe. Beneath the sky live not</span>
<span class="i0">A people dearer to the heart of Carle,</span>
<span class="i0">Save those of France, the conquerors of realms.</span>
<span class="i0">The Count Ogier de Danemarche, the brave,</span>
<span class="i0">Will lead—What beauty sits upon their brows!</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span></div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_221">CCXXI.</a></h4><div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">Now has the Emp'ror Carle three squadrons; Naimes</span>
<span class="i0">The Duke, then forms the fourth with truly brave</span>
<span class="i0">Barons from Allemagne, who left La Marche.</span>
<span class="i0">These, twenty thousand count, so all report;</span>
<span class="i0">Well furnished with good steeds and arms; for dread</span>
<span class="i0">Of death in battle they will never yield.</span>
<span class="i0">Herman the Duke of Thrace, their chief, will die</span>
<span class="i0">Before he guilty proves of cowardice.</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span></div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_222">CCXXII.</a></h4><div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">Duke Naimes and Joseran the Count, have formed</span>
<span class="i0">The fifth of Normans, twenty thousand men,</span>
<span class="pagenum">[158]</span><span class="i0">Say all the Franks. Their arms are bright, and fleet</span>
<span class="i0">Their steeds. These welcome death ere they succumb.</span>
<span class="i0">None under Heav'n more valiant in the fight.</span>
<span class="i0">Richard the old will lead them on the field,—</span>
<span class="i0">And with his trenchant lance will bravely strike.</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span></div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_223">CCXXIII.</a></h4><div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">Composed of Bretons the sixth squadron was:</span>
<span class="i0">Full forty thousand chevaliers are they;</span>
<span class="i0">Barons in mien when mounted thus, each lance</span>
<span class="i0">In rest, its pennon rolled. Their lord is named</span>
<span class="i0">Oedun: These led by Nevelon the Count,</span>
<span class="i0">Tedbald de Reins and the Marchis Othon—</span>
<span class="i0">"My people guide," said Carle; "in ye my trust."</span>
<span class="i13">Aoi.</span></div></div>
<h4> <a id="St_224">CCXXIV.</a></h4><div class="poem"><div class="stanza">
<span class="i0">King Carle has now six squadrons on the field.</span>
<span class="i0">Barons d'Alverne and Peitevins Duke Naimes</span>
<span class="i0">Has mustered in the seventh. They may count</span>
